Intro 1 - Meet Charlie Morley

For many people, when they think of lucid dreaming, being conscious in their dreams, they just think it's for fun. And I could fly about or I could have sex with movie stars and stuff like that. It's like, yeah, you can do that in lucid dreams. And if you want to, that's totally fine. So although I often say the first two years of my lucid dreaming were just spent on skateboarding, actually, I did have a really profound set of lucid dreams when I was about 17. Before I got into Buddhism, I was really, really wild. Drugs and gangs and all this kind of stuff. And when I was 17, I had a drugs overdose. And I had a classic near -death experience. Life flashing before my eyes, tunnel of light. But I came to the end of it, and it opened up into this huge, infinite blackness. And then in this infinite blackness, there was a voice. And it was my voice, like my voice, my accent. And it said, do you want to live? Or do you want to die? And I remember in the near -death experience just going, live, like screaming it. After that near -death experience, I know some people have like really amazing insights after NDEs. I didn't. I had panic attacks. I had nightmares. I was questing reality. I was deeply traumatized by the experience. So I'm having these terrible nightmares, and I remember in the books I'd read on lucid dreaming, they always had a chapter on how lucid dreaming could be used to treat nightmares. There was quite a lot of scientific research, even back then, 20 years ago. In the first three of these nightmares, where I'm basically reliving the near -death experience, I became lucid, but I couldn't face it. I just had to wake up. I bailed. But the fourth one... i managed to get lucid and i go okay i'm dreaming i'm dreaming i know this isn't real i know this is just my trauma playing out so i turned to face the trauma rather than getting out the dream i turned to face it and the dream was integrated and my nightmares stopped like four months of post -traumatic stress disorder nightmares stopped after one lucid dream That's when I really woke up to the power of lucid dreamings. This led me on a path of training with some of the most amazing Buddhist masters alive, teaching me the ancient practices of lucid dreaming and dream yoga. Eventually, they asked me to start teaching, and that's what I spent the last 12 years doing, teaching people how to wake up in their dreams as a way to wake up to life. Since then, I've written four books on the subject and run workshops and retreats around the world. More recently, I've started giving lucid dreaming lectures at some places I thought I never would. such as cambridge university oxford university and even the ministry of defense in london but what's happened in the last 10 years is that science has caught up with what the ancient traditions already knew that lucid dreaming is one of the most powerful transformative mind training exercises in existence that can radically shift the perspective we have in the waking state by training in our dreams so to be able to share it with people to be part of this movement brings me joy And I'm passionate about it because it's changed my life and I've seen it transform the lives of others. So I ask you to be part of this movement with me.

Week 1: Being Aware of Your Dreams

LESSON 1 – What is Lucid Dreaming?
--------------------
<v ->Hello, dreamers. My name is Charlie Morley.</v> And I've spent the past 12 years teaching thousands of people of all ages and abilities how to lucid dream. It's what I was put on this Earth to do. And I love to do it. So as you can see, I'm excited to be journeying with you on this 30-day Quest, a deep dive into the depths of your own mind. Taking this course may well change your entire life and will definitely change 1/3 of it. So I ask you before we begin, are you ready to wake up? You are? Then let's begin. Come join me on a journey into dreaming. Let's begin with a definition. What exactly is a lucid dream? A lucid dream is a dream in which you know you're dreaming as the dream is happening. So you're sound asleep, dreaming away, but within the dream, you had an aha moment of lucid awareness. You know that you're dreaming. Once you know that you're dreaming within the dream, you can interact, choreograph and direct the dream at will, choosing to do whatever you like. You can fly around. You can have sex with movie stars, yeah. But more importantly, you can also integrate trauma, rewire your brain and heal your mind from the inside out. In a lucid dream, you've not woken up. You're still sound asleep. If you want to know what a lucid dreamer looks like while they're having a lucid dream, they look like this. You are out for the count. But while you're out for the count, you are gaining access to the most powerful virtual reality generator in existence: the human mind. The first thing that strikes people about lucid dreaming is how realistic it is. It's not actually that dreamy at all. It's as real as this. You know that old saying, "Pinch yourself to see if you're dreaming"? In a lucid dream, if you pinch yourself, you'll just feel the pain of the pinch, even though you know that the dream fingers that are pinching and the dream arm that are being pinched don't exist. They're all a hallucination of your mind. A lucid dream is an experience of hyper reality. Now that may sound like an over-exaggeration, but bear with me on this one. Right now your experience of waking reality is limited by your five senses being filtered through the sixth. Perhaps in the waking state, your eyes aren't that good. Perhaps in the waking state, your ears aren't that good. Your experience of reality is limited by the sense organs. However, in a lucid dream, those limits are removed. This is why in a lucid dream, people often say, "How come I could see every little bit "of detail on that blade of grass? "How come in a lucid dream, "I could hear every aspect of that music?" Because in the lucid dream, you aren't seeing through your eyes, you're seeing through your mind. In the lucid dream, you aren't hearing through your ears, you're hearing through your mind. Of course, this is why lucid dreaming sex can be so seductive because in the lucid dream, you're not having an orgasm, you're having a mindgasm. Just last month I was teaching in Czech Republic and I had this really powerful lucid dream in which I integrated a shadow aspect, more about that later. But after I'd done the shadow integration, I opened a room and there was an office. And I thought, "Oh, wow. "Maybe it's like the office of my mind. "Maybe the CEO of my brain lives in here." But it was empty. So I thought I'll just explore. I'll just kind of look around. So I go over to the desk and there's all these plants like office plants. And I go and I touch the leaves and I'm thinking, I know this leaf doesn't exist, but it feels so realistic. And then I saw on the top of the leaf there was dust. And I thought, I know my body's asleep in bed right now in the waking state. I know this has all a three dimensional projection in my mind. And yet my dreaming mind has even taken the time to put in the detail of dust on the leaves. It's a really mind blowing experience. And an experience that most people have had at some point in their life, but only about 25% of people have lucid dreams regularly. My job is to put you guys into that 25%. Lucid dreaming is a learnable skill. And in this Quest, we will learn how to do it. But before we do that, let's learn what it's not. Number one, a lucid dream is not a half-awake-half-asleep state. As I said before, in the lucid dream, you are sound asleep, deep in REM, rapid eye movement, dreaming sleep. Number two, a lucid dream is not just a really vivid dream. Lucid dreams can be really vivid but it's not the vividness that defines it. It's the reawakening of reflective awareness. The aha moment. And number three, a lucid dream is not an outer body experience. In an outer body experience, a genuine one, your consciousness has left the confines of the physical body. It is outside of your mind. However, in a lucid dream, the mind has flipped inwardly and is experiencing consciously the totality of its own projection. So a lucid dream is a dream in which you know you're dreaming as you're dreaming, that's it. And once that happens, you gain access to a three dimensional construct of your own psychology. Literally walking about or flying about your own mind. There is so much bullshit out there about lucid dreaming: on forums, social media, and, even worse, just in people's heads. So let's do some quick myth-busting before we go any further. Myth number one, it will make you tired. No. Lucid dreaming occurs in non restful REM dreaming sleep. In many stages of sleep, the brain and the body are resting, but not in REM. REM uses a lot of blood flow. In fact, in REM dreaming, you're using so much blood flow, that you're actually burning calories. So dreaming is not restful sleep. Becoming lucid within your dreams actually makes it more restful, not less. There's also some interesting studies there that have shown that once people become lucid, their brain often moves into gamma wave. Now gamma waves are usually associated with those monks who've done over 10,000 hours of meditation. So isn't it strange that once you become lucid, the brain goes into a similar state? Of course, the Buddhists who've been practicing this stuff for a thousand years, they don't think it's strange at all. They say, yeah, because once you become lucid, you are in a state of meditation. And the third thing to know, is that whatever you do in the lucid dream, the brain thinks you're actually doing it. So if you spend your lucid dream having fun, then oxytocin is released into the bloodstream in exactly the same way as it would be when you're awake. So most people wake up feeling not tired, but elated, full of the joy of having a lucid dream. Myth number two, it's messing with the unconscious. OK, every time we have a dream, the unconscious is trying to communicate with us. So surely once we become a lucid and we increase that line of communication, the unconscious would be pleased. And it is. The unconscious is so happy to have you conscious within it because now it can actually talk to you directly. The dreamer wants to communicate with you and lucid dreaming is the most direct form of communication there is. Let us look at the percentages on this. If you have an eight hour sleep cycle, you have five dream periods a night. So even if you have a lucid dream every night of your life, by the way, come and teach me if you can do that, 'cause I'm not at that level. Even if you get lucid every night of your life, 80% of your dream experience will be non-lucid. So for those who are having one lucid dream a week, which is a pretty good average by the way, then 97% of your dreams will be non-lucid. So don't worry about lucid dreaming messing with the unconscious mind. Still, 97% of the time your unconscious can do what it likes. Myth number three, "It will make me crazy". No, lucid dreaming will make you sane. It is used as a tool for treating mental health issues. And there's even a form of therapy called lucid dreaming therapy which is having great success in teaching post-traumatic stress disorder in veterans. Surely what's really crazy is hallucinating for two and a half hours every night that we call dreams and believing it to be real. That's what happens in our normal lucid dreams. So in many ways, lucid dreaming is an anti-psychotic, because at least you know you're dreaming as you're dreaming. Myth number four, lucid dreaming is dream control. Okay, for the uninitiated lucid dreamer, it can seem that once you become lucid, you're controlling the dream and at a subjective level, you are. Let's say you become lucid and you choose to fly through the sky. You're controlling how fast you go. You're controlling your distance. You're controlling your velocity. But what's controlling the trees that you fly over? What's controlling the dream characters walking in and out of the buildings that you're flying across? Although, we can come to a level of subjective control, the vast majority of the dream is out of our control. Just as no sailor controls the sea, so too does no lucid dreamer control the dream. Myth number five, only special people can do it. OK, kids lucid dream naturally. Not every kid every night, but Harvard studies have shown that most children report lucid dreams as part of their childhood development. So just as the artist Picasso once said, "Every child is born an artist. "The problem is how to remain one, once we grow up." So too it is with lucid dreaming. Lucid dreaming is your birthright. You've been doing it since a kid and now is the time to learn how to do it again. The two most important mental qualities that we need to learn to lucid dream are ones that many of us had as a kid too: enthusiasm and determination. If you've got those, you got everything you need to learn to lucid dream. ♪ These are your actions for today. If you haven't yet, introduce yourself to the Tribe. And share with them your current experience, if any, of lucid dreaming. And share how many of those myths you were aware of before. I'd also like you to talk to your friends and family about lucid dreaming. Ask them if they've ever had a lucid dream. So that's day one done, dreamers. Congratulations for taking your first step onto the path of lucidity. Tomorrow we'll learn our first lucid dreaming technique. Explore the neuroscience of lucid dreaming and learn how it can be used to change our brain while we sleep. ♪

LESSON 2 – Remember Your Dreams with Dream Recall
--------------------
<v ->Yesterday, we covered the basics.</v> Today, we're going to explore how lucid dreaming actually changes your brain while you sleep. Although we think of dreams as a psychological experience because they are created out of our own psychology, once we become lucid, they absolutely become a neurological experience. And one in which we can literally rewire our brains while we sleep. Lucid dreaming science takes us all the way back to 1975 at Hull University in the UK. When lucid dreaming first became verified as a scientific phenomena of REM dreaming sleep. Before we learn how you can use lucid dreaming to change your brain, let's explore the neurology of lucid dreaming. When we're having a non-lucid dream, the back part of the brain is highly active. The visual cortex, the brain stem, the occipital lobe, but the front part of the brain, the prefrontal cortex, has very little blood flow. Now the scientists tell us that our sense of self, our sense of agency, our sense of "I am having experience", are linked and located in the prefrontal cortex. This is why in a non-lucid dream, you can dream you're other people. You can dream you're a man if you're a woman, you can dream you're a child if you're an adult because your sense of self is so much more flexible. A few years ago, I had a dream that I was the Queen of England, true story. I was standing in front of a long queue of people. I was wearing a pastel pink dress and this little matching hat and I was shaking people's hands and saying, "Hello, how'd you do and what do you do?" This long line of people. Now in that dream, because the prefrontal cortex was switched off, my sense of, "I am Charlie having experience" was offline, I happily believed that I was the Queen of England. Charlie had never existed. I had always been the Queen of England and always would be the Queen of England. Then my alarm clock went off in the waking state. I woke up, the prefrontal cortex came back online and I went, oh, I was dreaming I was the Queen of England, I'm actually Charlie. Now in a lucid dream, that same process of the reactivation of the prefrontal cortex occurs while you're still sleeping. So the front part of the brain lights up, you go, "Aha, I'm having a dream", and now you know who you are within the dream. Once you're lucid, the brain doesn't think you're imagining it or dreaming it, it thinks you're actually doing it because once the prefrontal cortex activates, the brain thinks you're awake. This opens up one of the most fascinating aspects of lucid dreaming, it's called neuroplasticity. Neuroplasticity describes the brain's potential to rewire itself in favor of a newly learnt skill. Essentially, the more you use a certain brain network, the more blood flow it gets and the deeper the grooves become in that brain network. Now in non-lucid dreaming, neuroplasticity is not engaged. So you can spend your whole night dreaming about soccer, in the morning you're probably not going to be any better at soccer. However, once you become lucid and the prefrontal cortex becomes reactivated, neuroplasticity is engaged. This means in the lucid dream, you can literally rewire your brain while you sleep. If you choose to practice soccer in your lucid dream, you may well wake up being better at soccer. And we're going to look at some of those exact same studies very soon. Here's a nice example to explain how neuroplasticity works. I've got a piece of paper here and I'm going to see how quickly I can fold it. Okay, quite quick, but nothing special. If I undo the paper and I try a second time, look how much quicker that was, why? Two main reasons, number one, I'd done it before and number two, the grooves were already there. That explains neuroplasticity. There are so many studies that have looked at the neuroplasticity aspect of lucid dreaming but here are some of the coolest. The University of Lincoln in the UK did a study that proved that lucid dreamers are better at problem solving. In the lucid dream, you get so much blood flow to these parts of the brain that see laterally, that think outside the box, that in the waking state the brain receives the same messages and people who have regular lucid dreams are proven to be better at creative problem solving. A study from the Max Planck Institute of Psychiatry, found that lucid dreamers actually have bigger brains. They have more gray matter in the anterior prefrontal cortex, thus making this part of the brain larger and making the lucid dreamers in the waking state, better at self-reflection. Now I know size doesn't matter guys but having a bigger brain is pretty cool. Lucid dreaming has a long history of being used by athletes to prepare for their athletic discipline. But before we look at how lucid dreaming can help train the body, let's look at how the waking state can do the same. A Cleveland Clinic Foundation study, found that fully awake subjects who imagined lifting heavy weights with their arms for 15 minutes a day, for five days a week, for 12 weeks in total, increased their bicep strength by up to 13.5% and the strength gain lasted for three months. This wasn't in a lucid dream, this was just waking state visualization. Now the limit of waking state visualization is that it's dependent on the subject's ability to visualize. If you can visualize really strongly, the gains increase. If you can't visualize that well, they decrease, but in the lucid dream, the playing field is leveled because in a lucid dream, you are in the most complete, three dimensional visualization you will ever experience. So once you apply visualized techniques within the lucid dream, they work with 100% efficacy. There've been loads of scientific studies looking at how lucid dreaming can be used to increase sports performance. These studies go way back to the 80's and all the way up to modern day ones at Heidelberg Uni. One of the latest, used 800 German athletes. Taught them to lucid dream and concluded that lucid dreams have a great potential for the athletes to use as a training method. What were they doing exactly? They were becoming lucid in their dreams and then practicing their athletic discipline in the dreams. This led to waking state performance that was measurable in a scientific lab. There was a study done in 2019, that wanted people who were good at lucid dreaming and who were also good at martial arts. So they asked me to be involved. We had to go into the lucid dream and practice a very specific kick sequence, a kickboxing kick sequence. And in the waking state, they tested us to see whether we got better. 81.3% of the participants got better at martial arts by practicing in their lucid dreams. Embarrassingly, I was one of the percentile that didn't get any better. Maybe I got it wrong, but 81.3% got better by training in their lucid dreams. If this stuff isn't shocking you, it should be. This means you can actually increase waking state experience while you sleep. "But so what? "I'm not into sport", you might say. Okay, but the reason I give these sports studies are because they have huge implications on how the mind can affect the body. They prove beyond doubt that lucid dream rehearsal leads to physical waking state changes and results. Think of the implications for public speaking. I want to tell you a story about my friend Leah. Leah is a clinical hypnotherapist, who's done lucid dreaming training with me. Before one of her final exams, she was nervous and she decided to have a lucid dream to work with her fear of public speaking. In the lucid dream, she called out to meet her fear, an embodiment of the fear appeared within the lucid dream. She embraced the fear, showed it love and she felt all of her anxiety dissolve. The next day, she gave a killer presentation. Maybe it's the integration of limiting habits that we can use lucid dreaming for. I had one gentleman come on a course about five years ago who had OCD, you know, very scared of germs. Didn't like to shake people's hands or touch people. He felt that this was really limiting him in his life. And he said, maybe I'd go into the lucid dream and call out, "I get rid of my OCD! I get rid of my OCD!" I said to him, you could do that but I'd advise something else. We don't want to highlight what we want to get rid of, we want to step in to how we want to be. How do you do that? I said, well maybe in the lucid dream, do what you're scared of doing, in the lucid dream, walk up and start shaking the hands of other dream characters, go and hug them knowing that they don't exist, you don't exist and the germs definitely don't exist. But remember your brain won't know that, your brain will think you're awake and actually facing the fear that's limiting you in your life. There's even some burgeoning research into using lucid dreaming for re-stimulating nervous system networks in people with spinal cord injuries. This is a very powerful potential. It's just burgeoning research, but in the same way as virtual reality is being used to help re-stimulate these networks, so too might lucid dreaming be able to do the same. And finally, there's the potential of placebo healing within the lucid dream. Because the power of visualization is so intense within the lucid dream, and because the power of the placebo is enhanced so much because you are literally inside your mind, there is the potential for healing physical ailments through lucid dreaming. Again, there's no scientific studies on this. All of it is anecdotal, but I'm currently applying for funding to do one of the first studies to prove that you can have inflammation markers come into the bloodstream after a lucid dream healing, thus proving that we can do it. I'll give much more information about this as the course progresses, but for now, let me at least plant the seed. There is the potential to treat physical ailment through lucid dreaming. Remember, the main thing is that the brain doesn't think you're dreaming in a lucid dream. It thinks you're actually doing it. Not that you're imagining it not that you're visualizing it, but that you are actually doing it. That's why it has such a powerful effect on our brain networks. Now, let's look at lucid dreaming as a tool for trauma integration. I've taught lucid dreaming to hundreds of people suffering from post-traumatic stress disorder, including military veterans, victims of terrorist attacks and those who experience abuse during childhood. And I've seen firsthand just how powerful lucid dream training can be, for integrating trauma. A 2009 European Science Foundation meeting, described lucid dreaming as such an effective treatment for post-traumatic stress disorder, that there is the potential for treating nightmares by learning to dream lucidly. Could this herald a time when lucid dreaming is prescribed in just the same way as mindfulness meditation is now? I hope so, there've been so many studies looking at the powerful effects that lucid dreaming can have on PTSD nightmares. And one of the latest ones that I was involved in, was at Swansea University in the UK. This study found that people who could train their minds to become lucid in their nightmares, had a great decrease in the nightmare frequency. In fact, lucid dreaming is such an effective treatment for nightmares, that in 2019, I gave a talk at the Ministry of Defense in London. It's a bit like the UK version of the Pentagon, about the power of lucid dreaming, to 300 military defense staff. 10 years ago, that would have been unthinkable. But now the science is in, lucid dreaming is powerful. This stuff really works, but how does it work exactly? Let's take an example of one of the veterans that I worked with. When he came back from Iraq, he had terrible post-traumatic stress disorder nightmares, which manifested as him being back in Iraq. When he woke up from that nightmare, he had the same feeling in his body as if he was actually there. He was sweating, heart palpitations, all of that. Once he trained himself lucid dreaming and he could learn to become a lucid within that nightmare, he could go, "Oh, I'm not really back in Iraq, "I'm dreaming I'm back in Iraq". In fact, now my body's safe in bed and this is just a hallucination of the trauma that occurred to me. That in itself was enough to pick up the needle on the stuck record of post-traumatic stress disorder and allowing the trauma that was creating the nightmare, to flow through his mind. So simply the act of becoming lucid in a nightmare, can be enough for many people to unstick them from the stuck state of PTSD. With the other veterans that I've worked with, some of them yes, did learn to lucid dream, but for others, even though they didn't become lucid, their nightmares started to become integrated, simply by learning about how they sleep, by depathologizing nightmares, knowing that they're not an attack from the mind but a sign of a healing mind and a dream that is shouting. We'll learn more about nightmares in week four. And you'll see that just opening up to the potential of what they are, even before you get lucid in them, can still have deep healing potential. And the reason why trauma integration in lucid dreaming is so powerful, is again, because the brain thinks you have actually faced the fear in real life. Once you're lucid, the brain doesn't think you're dreaming, the brain thinks you're awake. That's why it's so effective. For those of you listening who don't have heavy trauma, maybe you've got mild phobia. Let's say you're scared of spiders for example. Lucid dreaming can absolutely be used to integrate fear of spiders. How, you become lucid. "Okay, I'm dreaming, I'm dreaming." Don't call out "Spiders now", as one of my students did and everything in the dream turned to spiders, he freaked out and woke up. But you could go, "Oh, I'm dreaming, I'm dreaming. "Right, what did I want to do? "I wanted to integrate my fear of spiders". So then in the dream, you could have the intention or you could call out, friendly spider now. Usually in the lucid dream, reality manifests from a thought or intention, so a spider would appear. You could then pick the spider up in your hand. Remember it will feel incredibly realistic. You can allow the spider to crawl up your arm and yeah, you'll feel scared because it will seem so real but you'll know, "Hang on, the spider doesn't exist". But at the same time the brain thinks it does. So the brain thinks you are actually allowing a spider to crawl up your arm fearlessly. So it starts to rewire itself in favor of this newly learned approach to spiders and in that way, we can start to integrate our fear the spiders at a deep neurological level. I'm sure stories about the awesome potential of lucid dreaming for physical training and trauma integration inspired you. They still inspire me actually, every time I retell them. So I asked you, can you channel that inspiration into our first lucid dreaming technique? Which is dream recall. Dream recall is one of the most important aspects of lucid dream training. Some say that until you regularly remember your dreams, you might be having lucid dreams every night without realizing it. It's a possibility but actually it's far more likely that if you don't remember your dreams, you probably aren't going to be having many lucid ones. Why, because the more conscious you are of your dreams, the easier it'll be to become conscious within your dreams. Based on an eight hour sleep cycle, most people have four or five dream periods every night but not everybody remembers these. I believe the main reason is simply because they don't try to remember them. Think about it, when did you last really try to remember your dreams? Well, here are some top tips on how to do so. Tip number one, remind yourself that you do dream. Every night you are dreaming, even if you don't remember it. This is because dreaming is vital to the human brain because it's linked to our survival and our evolution. The only way to stop the human brain from dreaming is a stroke or a heavy head injury and even then within a few weeks, the brain will rewire itself, so you're dreaming again. So know that you are dreaming, perhaps you simply don't remember them. Dreaming is about trauma integration and re-consolidation of memory, so it's really important for the brain to do it and that's why it prioritizes dreaming every night. So you do dream, it's simply a question of, do you remember your dreams? Number two, fall asleep with an affirmation in the hypnagogic state, to remember your dreams. What is the hypnagogic state? The hypnagogic state is a transitional state of mind that the brain passes through as it's moving into sleep. So you're lying in bed, your eyes are closed, you're feeling very drowsy. You're not fully asleep yet, but you're getting there. That's the hypnagogic state. And as you can see from the term hypna-gogic, it's close to a state of hypnosis. And if you look at the brainwaves of someone in the hypnagogic state, they are identical to those as someone in a hypnotic trance, deep alpha and theta. So for this technique, you harness the power of the hypnagogic that hypnotic state that you pass through as you fall asleep, by reciting a hypnotic suggestion to remember your dreams. The suggestion is this, "Tonight I remember my dreams, "I have excellent dream recall. "Tonight, I remember my dreams, "I have excellent dream recall". Recite that 21 times as you fall asleep. I'll be offering you a guided audio meditation, guiding you through that 21 recitation, which you can use as you fall asleep tonight. Number three, the first thought upon awakening needs to be, "How am I feeling? "What was I doing, where was I?" Go straight back to the dream as soon as you wake up. Remembering a dream though, is a bit like catching a feather. Imagine a feather falling. If I grab for it, the feather stays out of reach. To catch a feather, we need to place our hand just beneath it and catch it gently, so too with our dreams. Tip number four, work backwards. When you wake up, even if you can just find the last image from the dream, you can work backwards, picking up piece by piece by piece of the dream, until eventually you've unraveled the whole narrative. Tip number five, don't move too much. Have you noticed how you can be lying on this side, dreaming away and then you roll over to hit the alarm clock and suddenly the dream memory goes. There's a theory that it's to do with the movement of your cerebral fluid in your brain but whatever it is, once you move a lot, the dream often shakes out of your memory. However, if you're dreaming like this, you roll over to hit the alarm clock but then you return to your original sleeping position, you may find the memory returns. And the final tip, once you remember your dreams, be sure to write them down. Tomorrow, we'll learn exactly how to keep a dream diary but until then, tonight just make some notes. Enjoy and remember your dreams. And now your actions for today. Firstly, practice dream recall, either using the guided meditation audio or by doing the recurring affirmation by yourself, as you fall asleep tonight. Secondly, I'd like you to write down your dreams once you remember them. As I said, tomorrow we'll learn how to keep a dream diary but for tonight, just write some notes. And finally, share your experience with the Tribe. Which skill would you like to improve through lucid dream training? See you tomorrow.

LESSON 3 – Create Your Dream Diary

<v ->Today, we're going to be adding the staple ingredient</v> to our lucid dreaming meal, by learning exactly how to keep a dream diary. We'll also be exploring the psychological benefits of lucid dreaming, so that you can appreciate just how practical lucid dreaming is for life optimization, and for not so much hacking your unconscious mind, but absolutely for befriending it. Now that we've covered how lucid dreaming affects our brain and how it can be used for life rehearsal and trauma integration, let's explore how it can be used as a self-development tool for powerful real-life manifestation. I first got into lucid dreaming in my teens, and by my mid-20s, I was pretty proficient at it. It was never seen as a career path, though. So when I was 25, and my Buddhist teacher first asked me to start giving talks about lucid dreaming, I felt really out of my depth. At the time he asked me to start giving talks, I was actually making my living as a moderately successful rapper in a hip-hop group. I used to put on break dance events, perform in hip-hop groups, that kind of stuff, a far cry from lucid dreaming in the Buddhist scene. But my teacher had faith in me and he asked me to start giving talks. So although he had faith, I didn't have much faith in myself, and I started having these recurring nightmares or anxiety dreams every time I was lucid. One of these manifested in a lucid dream where a huge tidal wave was coming towards me. I became lucid, I saw the tidal wave, and I thought, that's probably my anxiety about being asked to teach. So how do you hug a tidal wave? Well, I can't hug it, but I'm going to kind of surf it. So I allowed the wave to come and I bodysurfed it down the street. Now often, in a lucid dream, once you get the point that the unconscious mind is trying to highlight, you wake up, as if the dreaming mind wants you to reflect on the insight you received. But once the tidal wave crashed, I was still in there, and I thought, oh, maybe the integration isn't complete. So I thought, I'm going to call out a question to the lucid dream. So I called out to the lucid dream, "What should I do with my life? Should I do Throwdown," the name of the break dance group that I was in, "or should I be a lucid dreaming teacher?" As soon as I called out that question, boom! The dream transformed, and I was suddenly in a small house on the ground floor at a cocktail party. And I'm thinking, what the hell? Am I supposed to be a cocktail waiter or something? And I walk around this party and I realize that some of the characters, they're almost kind of larger-than-life. They've got this huge energy. And I realize, they're my internal archetypes. The first one I saw was kind of dressed like a Buddhist monk, but in white robes. And I went up to him, fully lucid, and I said, "What should I do with my life?" And he looked me, bowed, and said, "You must be a lucid dreaming teacher. That's how you can truly benefit." And I was like, "Whoa, that's so cool!" Then I looked to my left and I saw this character who was obviously my inner hedonism. He looked like I did as a teenager, with this long ponytail, he had his top off, and he was holding a bottle of vodka. And I went over to him and said, "Hey, man, what should I do with my life?" And he gave me the bottle of vodka and went, "Throwdown, Throwdown rocks!" So obviously my inner spiritual guide wanted me to be a lucid dreaming teacher. My inner hedonist wanted me to stay in the hip-hop world. I then asked two other characters. They both said to be a lucid dreaming teacher. And I thought, okay, I've got the message. But again, I didn't wake up. So I did something strange. I left the dream. I found the front door, walked out of the house, and continued down the street where the tidal wave had occurred. I thought, why aren't I waking up yet? Surely I've got the message! And then I turned back 'round to see the house, and as I turn around, all of those archetypes I'd spoken to were watching me walk down the street through the window. And when I turned around they saw me went, "Good luck! You can do it, you can do it!" And I woke up with this big rush of emotion. As I realized that although I didn't have faith in myself, there was something in me that did. There was something in me that said, this is your path. This is what you are meant to do. Now, it'd be really cool in the story if the next day I gave up the hip-hop world and suddenly became a lucid dreaming teacher. It didn't work quite like that, but within about six months, I'd found a way to wrap up the work in the music industry and start running lucid dreaming workshops full-time. And now 12 years later, that's what I do. And it all started from that one lucid dream. So if you're thinking, where's my life going? How can I find my life path? You can absolutely use the lucid dream to ask that question and receive that answer. The next case study comes from my friend Manny from New York. He's a life coach and he wanted to use lucid dreaming to go really deep into his own self-development. One of the questions he'd been looking at and working on with his therapist was his greatest fear. And he realized that the lucid dream, because you are inside your unconscious mind, it would be a great place to get the answer to that question. So he became lucid, "Oh, I'm dreaming!" And he called out to the dream, "Show me my greatest fear!" Suddenly a woman appeared from a pool of water. She looked at him and you said, "Being loved. Being loved is your greatest fear." Manny said the insight from that one lucid dream was more insightful than the years of therapy he'd been in. So the lucid dream gives us access to the truth of who we are, and the truth of the parts of ourself that we'd rather not look at. Our greatest fears, our greatest loves, our greatest challenges. All of this we can work with through lucid dreaming. My friend Sergio Magana is a practitioner within the Toltec Mexihca shamanic tradition. He uses his lucid dreams to plant the seeds of an idea that will manifest in his waking state. A few years ago, he had a lucid dream where he planted the seed of, a star will come into my life. Within a few months, he gets a call from an actor's agent named Michelle Rodriguez, made famous for her roles in "The Fast and the Furious" movies. They eventually became friends, started working together, and have now even written a book together. That's the power of lucid dream self-inception. So just like the movie "Inception," where they plant ideas in the minds of others, in the lucid dream, we can absolutely plant ideas in our own mind, which will lead to waking-state changes and optimization of our life. Let's now look at how to keep a dream diary. If you want to learn how to lucid dream properly, then keeping a dream diary is essential. Not only because by writing down your dreams, you solidify the memory of the unconscious dream into your conscious awareness, but also because every time you write down a dream, you reinforce the habit of viewing the dream as something valuable. This then creates a beneficial feedback loop, 'cause once you see your dreams as valuable, you'll naturally start to recall them with more ease. There are so many benefits to keeping a dream diary. But here are some of my favorites. Once you know what you dream about, you're more likely to know that you're dreaming while you're dreaming. For example, if you keep a dream diary for a week or so, by the end of the week, you might notice patterns. Oh, I often dream of being on a beach or I often dream of my dead grandma, or I often dream of being naked in public. By being aware of what you're dreaming about, you're then more likely to be aware of your dreams while you're in them, and that, of course, is the essence of lucid dreaming. Another great benefit of lucid dreaming is that it gives you insight into your own mind. If you want to look at the internal environment of your mind, you don't need to ask a therapist. You need to look at your dream diary. People spend a lot of money going to therapists saying, "What's going on in here?" You want to know what's going on in here? Look to your dreams. Who are you secretly in love with? What traumas from childhood are still getting you down? What future events are you worried about? The answer to all those can be found in your own dream diary. One of the core benefits of keeping a dream diary is that it helps us to start to recognize our dream signs. We start to see distinct patterns emerging within our dreams. And once we see those patterns, recognize them and acknowledge them, we can start to plant the seeds that will lead to lucid dreams in the future. In the coming days, we'll learn how to find dream signs, but for now, know that just by keeping a dream diary, you start to open yourself up to that potential. Here are my five top tips for keeping a dream diary. Number one, whenever you wake up from a dream, recall as much of the dream as you can and then write it down in some way. You need to document it. It could be written, it could be drawn, it could be danced. However you do it, document the dream in some way, even if it's in the middle of the night when you've woken up for a pee. Number two, you don't need to record every tiny detail of your dream. You'll know what feels worth recording and what doesn't. Focus on the main themes and feelings, the general narrative, and any strange dream anomalies that you can recall. Number three, you don't need to spend half an hour every morning documenting your dreams. In fact, you'll be surprised how much you can write up in just 5 or 10 minutes. If you do have half an hour to spend writing up your dreams, brilliant. But I'm yet to find someone who can add an extra 30 minutes onto their daytime schedule and not start resenting it. So 5 or 10 minutes is more than enough. Number four, feel free to use a smartphone, a pen and pad, canvas, whatever you like, to document your dreams; make it your own. But the main thing is that you document them in some way. And tip number five, write down something in your dream diary, even if you can't remember any dreams. It's totally fine to write down, "Ah, I know I was dreaming, but I can't remember what about." That's great! It's authentic, it's real. And at least it doesn't set up a feeling of failure, of, oh, I didn't write anything in my dream diary. And my final tip, work from where you're at. There'll be some of you watching this quest who already remember three, four dreams a night. Brilliant, try and bring yourself to the next level and remember five or six. For other people watching this, they'll think, I haven't remembered a dream for 10 years! So if you just remember one fraction of the dream tonight, that's a massive breakthrough! So work from where you're at. Here are your actions for today. Number one is to write down your dreams in your dream diary. In fact, I'm going to ask you to do that every single morning for the next four weeks. Remember, you don't have to spend hours writing up your dreams, just 5 or 10 minutes is enough each morning. Keeping a dream diary is imperative if you want to learn how to lucid dream. And secondly, keep falling asleep reciting the dream recall affirmation that we learned yesterday. See you tomorrow, when we will explore the top 10 benefits of lucid dreaming, and you guys will start your very own Mindvalley Tribe Dream Circle. See you tomorrow.

Combine that with over 10 years of teaching lucid dreaming, and as you can imagine, I've got a pretty good idea of some of the most beneficial reasons why people have lucid dreams. So here in no particular order are my top benefits of lucid dreaming. Trauma integration. My new book is all about how trauma and stress affect sleep. Shadow integration. It's said in the Gospel of Thomas, "If you bring forth what is within you, "what you bring forth will save you. "If you do not bring forth what is within you, "what you do not bring forth will destroy you." No better description of shadow integration have I ever come across. The shadow is a term popularized in the West by the Swiss psychiatrist, Carl Jung, used to describe the aspects of the unconscious mind which we have rejected, denied, or disowned. The shadow is the dark side of the human psyche. But crucially not dark, meaning bad, evil, or malign, dark meaning yet to be illuminated. In the lucid dream, you can actually meet your shadow side because your shadow side resides within the unconscious mind. Later in the Quest we'll learn exactly how to do that. But for now know this, shadow integration through lucid dreaming is one of the most powerful whys there is. As we mentioned before, there is the the potential to engage placebo effect healing through the lucid dream. The placebo effect works by the mind affecting the body. So if you engage the placebo effect within the mind it becomes mind within mind and has very powerful effects. There is burgeoning research into the potential for lucid dreaming being used to heal physical ailments. Many people say that lucid dream healing is just the placebo effects. Well, anyone who says just the placebo effect does not understand how the placebo effect works. The placebo effect is the only replicable element of every medical trial. It's so powerful that you can give someone a pill and tell them this is a placebo, there's nothing in it, but just the act of popping the pill, taking the water and swallowing it has such a powerful effect on the mind that healing can still occur. Placebo effect healing is insane. Within the lucid dream, it seems that we can harness the power of the placebo effect by engaging visualized healing within the dream. The wise man representing guidance, knowledge and wisdom, the mother representing nurturing, comfort, feminine. "And just like a powerful hypnotic suggestion "that will implant in your unconscious "and make changes your waking state." Now, luckily Antonio is way more creative than me. Actually he's quite sassy too. And his brain went, "Okay," and he woke up. Maybe it was a 30 second lucid dream in total. Within those 30 seconds Antonio seemed to implant such a powerful suggestion that he never smoked again. Two months later he emails again, says "I'm still not smoking." I'm then writing my second book so I decided to interview him and his boyfriend to see whether he was telling the truth for my next book. Turned out he was telling the truth, his boyfriend confirmed it. Antonio and his boyfriend, also called Charlie, said something interesting had happened too. There's an interesting phenomena found in stage hypnosis called "negative hallucination" where you can hypnotize someone to such a deep level they literally won't see what they've been hypnotized not to see. There's a theory that perhaps the suggestion that Antonio placed in his brain was so strong he literally couldn't see the word cigarettes written on the shopping list. So it seems like we can work with addiction as directly as Antonio did or as indirectly as working through trauma integration. For as Dr. Gabor Mate says "The link between addiction "and trauma is inarguable". And finally, the greatest why of all, having fun. I trained myself to lucid dream at 16 years old. That's like three years before I got into Buddhism, moved into the Buddhist temple, all that kind of stuff. So of course, a 16 year old with access to a huge virtual reality simulation, you can imagine what I used to do, a lot of skateboarding and a lot of sex. So I'm not here to judge. If you want to spend your lucid dreams having fun go for it. Oxytocin is a vagus nerve tonic. And so oxytocin release is really good for us. And oxytocin is released in exactly the same way in the lucid dream as if we were awake. So being happy in your dreams means happy in your life. Simply by becoming aware of these powerful why benefits of lucid dreaming, some of you might find that you start to have lucid dreams already. So before we get there, let's move on to the lucidity spectrum to learn about the different levels that lie within lucidity. Level one, pre-lucid. Pre-lucid is a term coined by the lucid dream researcher, Celia Green to describe the state of mind in which we critically question the reality of the dream. So in the pre-lucid state, suspicions arise that we might be dreaming usually after we become aware of some sort of bizarre dream anomaly. For example, we might be in the dream going, "Hang on, why am I talking to my dead grandma? "She died ages ago!" Sometimes pre-lucid leads to the light bulb moment of, "Wow, I'm dreaming," but often not. Often we just have the dreams where we're saying, "Wait, why am I naked in public, that's so weird!" And the dream continues. That's pre-lucid. Level two, semi-lucid. Here, we experience the aha moments of lucid awareness but then we might slip back and forth between lucidity and non-lucidity. We might be lucid one moment then become distracted and slip into non-lucid dreaming the next moment. Also semi-lucid is often used to describe low level lucidity where you might be lucid enough to know you're dreaming but not so lucid that you're choosing to direct or choreograph the dream. Level three, fully lucid. This is a state of fully conscious reflective awareness within the dream coupled with volitional interaction with the dreamscape and dream characters. With full lucidity, we can maintain awareness for the entirety of the dream period which may be an hour or more in length. Now this is cool, you might be thinking, "But where's time in lucid dreams?" In a non-lucid dream time's out the window. But once you become lucid and the prefrontal cortex is activated, your estimation of time is pretty much the same as in the waking state. So if it feels like you've been lucid dreaming for 30 seconds, you probably have. If it feels like you've been lucid for an entire hour, you probably have too. Imagine spending 60 minutes flying or walking around the internal environment of your own psychology. Many people believe that fully lucid is the highest possible level of lucidity. I disagree, there is a deeper level, level four, super-lucid. This is a term used to describe the state in which we have a level of awareness that surpasses full lucidity. Due to an experience of partial non-dual awareness. What does this mean? Well, the fundamental difference between fully lucid and super-lucid rests on a subtle but profound shift of perception. For example, in a fully lucid dream, I might become lucid, remember my dream plan, "Oh, I want to go surfing" and then think, "Okay, I'm in a room." So I find the door, I walk out the door, out into the space, put my hand up, fly through the sky, find a beach and go surfing, fully lucid. In a super-lucid dream though, I wouldn't walk through the door, I'd just go straight through the wall. I wouldn't fly to the beach, I'll just go "Beach, now." We stopped interacting with the dream as if the laws of physics apply. In fact with super-lucidity, often your level of consciousness is so high that you probably wouldn't bother going surfing. You'll probably just stand in the dream, experiencing the deep meditation of the non-dual awareness state. Witnessing dreams, this type of dream falls within the lucidity spectrum but doesn't quite fit into any of the other levels. In a witnessing dream, we experience lucid awareness but it's as if we're watching the dream on a screen. So we're not in the dream in the first person, we're kind of watching it from a third person. So we probably won't be able to interact or choreograph the dream, but we simply watch the dream happening from a removed perspective. Witnessing dreams are often associated with extended periods of meditation. So if you're doing a lot of meditation you might find you have a lot of witnessing dreams. Human beings have been discussing their dreams around the embers of last night's campfires since beginningless time. What I want us to do on this Quest is to start sharing our dreams in a dream circle with the other members of the Tribe. This means checking in at least a few times a week with other members of the Tribe to discuss the dreams you've been having. Checking in about how many dreams you can remember, how your dream recall's been going, how is it to keep the dream diary. And also simply about connecting emotionally by sharing your dreams with another. There's been an interesting study again from Swansea University that has shown that when people share their dreams in dream circles it actually leads to an increase in empathy. And that makes sense, right? 'Cause when you share your dreams with someone, you're not sharing who you believe yourself to be, you're sharing who you really are. Our hearts open when we share our dreams with another. So by engaging our Quest dream circles, I hope your heart opens too. And one more thing, simply by speaking your dreams out loud you pay homage to the dreamer, that part of the brain that creates and plays out your dreams. Every time you speak a dream, every time you share a dream, every time you write a dream, you're sending a message to your mind that says "Dreams are something important to me. "Dreams are something I value." And with that, the dream will start to manifest with more value too. Now for your actions for today. Number one, start a dream circle and share your dream experience with at least a few other members of the Tribe. Number two, keep up the dream recall affirmation and the dream diaries. So I hope today's session has left you feeling hyped up not only by the top 10 whys of lucid dreaming, but also by the lucidity spectrum. Because awareness of that spectrum means that you may already have been having lucid dream experiences. I'm sure some of you may have already been getting pre-lucid, right? Or maybe even fully lucid, if so, share it with your dream circle and celebrate it with the Tribe. Tomorrow we move on the most important lucid dreaming technique of the entire Quest, lucid dream planning. See you then, sweet dreams.

LESSON 5 – 3 Steps to Creating a Dream Plan
--------------------
<v ->Planning a lucid dream</v> is a lucidity technique in itself, because when we set a strong intention to do something in our first or next lucid dream, we not only start to attract the causes and conditions needed to make that dream manifest, but we also create an expectation of becoming lucid. And for lucid dreaming, expectation is very powerful. This technique used to go at the end of my books and courses, but now I put it at the beginning. Because as we learned yesterday, if you have a good reason why to lucid dream and a plan for how to get that dream to manifest, you don't need to go looking for lucid dreams, because lucid dreams will come looking for you. Dream Planning, whatever you plan to do in your lucid dream, should turn you on psychologically. It should make you salivate. It should really make you excited to go to sleep because you can't wait to have the lucid dream. Maybe you make a dream plan to meet you and a child and integrate childhood trauma. Maybe you make a dream plan to heal a physical ailment, to face a fear, to ask for career advice, to do your spiritual practice, whatever it is, make a plan. My advice is this. Even if you're a total beginner, when you make your dream plan, aim high. If you aim too high, your dream are simply won't comply or it will help you dial it down a little bit. So don't worry about going too far out. Go as far out as your imagination will allow you to. When I was in my mid 20s, I made a dream plan to visit Nirvana. Now I'm a card carrying Buddhist, and lived in a Buddhist center for seven years. So as you can imagine, the idea of Nirvana is a concept and idea that I'm very familiar with. So I thought, wouldn't it be cool if through the lucid dream I could actually go to or experience Nirvana. So I made my dream plan, I became lucid and in the lucid dream I called out, "Dewachen now!" Dewachen is a Tibetan word used to describe a Nirvana pure realm. So I get lucid and call out, "Dewachen now!" Now nothing happens. A second time, "Dewachen, take me to Dewachen now!" Now nothing happens. A third time, "Take me to Dewachen." A woman with a clipboard walks into the lucid dream straight up to me, looks down at the clipboard, looks up and says, "Dewachen, nah, you're not ready for that." Boom and I wake up. There was some aspect that my unconscious mind that knew that experiencing Nirvana, going to Dewachen, might blow my tiny 20 year old mind. So it dialed it down, it edited, it censored the dream for me. However, a few years later I did that same dream plan, and this time the woman with the clipboard didn't turn up and I did have that experience. That experience altered my life forever, because I realized if that's true, then maybe all the other stuff is true too. And then reality truly started to open up to me. Dream planning is done in three steps, plan, picture, sankalpa. Step one, the plan. Once you've decided what you want to do, check the top 10 whys for inspiration, begin to formulate your dream plan. Start with the sentence, "In my next lucid dream I," and then write a description of what you want to do once you're lucid. For example, maybe in your next lucid dream you want to heal a knee injury. So you write, In my next lucid dream, once I'm lucid, I do hands-on Reiki healing on my knee injury. As I placed my hands on my knee, colored light shoots out of the palm of my hands. I also call out an affirmation such as, "I am free of any and all non-beneficial disease. I am free of any and all non-beneficial disease. When I wake in the morning, the inflammation in my knee is greatly reduced." So there you go. There's a description of what you want to happen in the lucid dream, and the outcome you'd like to come from it. Step two, picture. Most of us dream in pictures and images. So draw a little picture of your dream plan and action as a way to get one step closer to the manifestation of that dream. Now I'm not much of an artist, so I just use like stick man and little pictures and speech bubbles. But if you are an artist, then go for it, do something beautiful, do something abstract, go as far out as you like. The main thing is, that you somehow pictorialize the idea of the lucid dream you want to have. Step three, sankalpa. Sankalpa is a Sanskrit word, which means will or intent. And when I use the term sankalpa here, I'm referring to a call to action. It should be a pithy statement that sums up the essence of your dream plan. So for example, if your dream plan has a complex description of how you intend to reduce inflammation in your knee and heal an ailment, then the sankalpa might be, "Healing my knee now" or "Healing energy now." It's the call to action that you engage once you're in the lucid dream, almost like a code word that unlocks the dream plan. So the dream plan can be a detailed as poetic and descriptive as you like, but the sankalpa should be short and sharp. Here are some examples of dream plans and action. A few years ago, I decided that I wanted to try and meet the essence of my divine feminine within the lucid dream. So I made a dream plan. In mind next lucid dream, I call out to meet the essence of my divine feminine. She appears in personified form. I embrace her with love and fully balanced both the female and male energies within my psychology. I wake up feeling elated and fully in connection with my divine feminine. Then the picture, was a bit rubbish, I just drew a little picture of me a little picture of the divine feminine, and then what thought was integration. The sankalpa was a simple as, "Divine feminine come to me." So about a week later, I become lucid in the dream. Oh, I'm dreaming. I remember what's my dream plan, to meet my divine feminine. So I call out in the lucid dream, "Divine feminine come to me, divine feminine, come to me." And rather than some sort of angel appearing or the mother Mary or some female Dakini embodiment of enlightened wisdom, this music started, ♪ ton ton ton ton ton ton ton ton ton ton ♪ Now, what the hell is that? Then suddenly these big double doors appeared. They swung open and three big guys walk through them. One white guy, one black guy, one Indian guy wearing a turban. They're really big, kind of like bounces at a nightclub. And the cool thing about a lucid dream, is that you can interpret the dream while you're in it. So in the lucid dream, I started to tell myself, "Oh, so maybe my divine feminine has manifested in this hyper masculine form. Oh, that's very interesting, that's really interesting, that the kind of gender dynamics there." But I was wrong. They were the entourage with the divine feminine. As they walk towards me these three big guys, the door swing open again and the music pumps up more, ♪ boom, boom boom, boom, boom, boom, boom, boom, boom ♪ And then through door comes my divine feminine. How did she look? It was freaking Beyonce. Walking through sassy as anything I was, "Oh my God my divine is Beyonce." Suddenly the dream changes. I'm on the floor, looking up on my knees, and on a couch, like a kind of a chaise launch thing is this Beyonce divine feminine figure, completely naked. Now looking back on the dream, it's strange there was no sexual energy there, but to be honest the power of the divine feminine was so strong, I kind of didn't project anything sexual onto her. I was too scared to do so, she was pure female power. And on my knees, I look up to her and I say, in the dream, "Are you my divine feminine?" She looks down at me, touches my face and says in an American Beyonce accent, "No honey, you are." Suddenly this huge amount of energy rushes through my body and I wake up in tears. Now I have no idea whether that was actually the full integration of my divine feminine, now I have no idea if your divine feminine will manifest the Beyonce too, but the reason I'm telling you this, is to see just how differently the dream manifest from the dream plan created. Remember in the dream plan, I thought it was going to be some sort of angel or mother Mary figure, in the end it was Beyonce. So we make our dream plan, it doesn't kind of force the mind into anything. It just makes a suggestion to the mind. When you make your dream plan, it's like a love letter, written to the unconscious saying, "I'd really love to do this in my next lucid dream." Sometimes the unconscious follows to the plan, sometimes it hide us on the plan. The second example I want to share with you is a much more serious case. There was a lady who came to one of my lucid dreaming immersive retreats. And on the first night she had a lucid dream. This is before we'd really learn any other lucid dream techniques, other than dream planning. In her dream plan, she had decided she wanted to call out to meet her seven year old self. When I asked her why, she said, "Because around the age of seven, that's when some abuse happened." And that now she was an adult, even though she knew it was strange to still feel it, she felt a sense of guilt that somehow she was in the wrong, that somehow it was her fault at the age of seven. So she thought that through the lucid dream, she might be able to reconnect with that little girl, embrace her, show her love and tell her it wasn't your fault, as a way to help integrate the trauma in the waking state. That night she became lucid, and she called out to the dream. "Seven-year-old self, come to me, seven-year-old self now." Now nothing happened. The dream blocked it. Some sort of intelligence within her dreaming mind, knew that perhaps to meet her seven-year-old self, would have retraumatized her. Some sort of intelligence knew, nah, we shouldn't go there. But when she called out a third time, "Seven-year-old self come to me", suddenly a door appeared in the lucid dream, and on the door was a sign, and the sign read, "Caution", what amazing symbolism from the intelligence of the dreaming mind? She was in the lucid dream and she thought to herself, well what would Charlie say to do? I was like, "Push the door, push the door." And she thought, "Okay, well if the door is locked, I'll take that as a symbolic representation that the trauma is locked behind a door and I shouldn't touch it. If the door swings open, I'll proceed with caution." So she pushes the door in the lucid dream. As soon as the door opens, suddenly a three story building manifests. On each story of the building, is a different symbolic representation of the abuse, on the ground floor is a room full of vomit because there was some of the abuse where she was forced eat cause she was sick. And she walked into this room fearlessly and she was so sweet she thought, "Oh, Charlie always talks about hugging things in your dreams, hugging your demons, hugging the monsters, because they're all parts of you. Show them love. How do I hug a room full of vomit?" She thought on our feet and said, "Well, it's all symbolic, so I'll hug it with my mind." So she walked in and called out affirmations. "I set you free it, it wasn't your fault, I set you free." And she had a huge emotional release. And the next day it was such a privilege to hear her telling that dreams to me in the dream circle. A few years later, when I was writing my book, "Dreaming Through Darkness", I wanted to include that dream as a case study. So I had to make contact with the young lady again. And she said, "Yeah, absolutely, you can use the dream, but I want you to tell people this, that dream didn't heal me of the childhood trauma, but what did happen, is after that dream all the blocks had stopped me going into therapy, talking to people about it. Being open to healing my trauma, was suddenly removed." She suddenly felt the lucid dream had removed those blocks and now she could move towards the journey to healing. In fact, the last time I spoke to her, she was about to have Christmas with some of the family members involved. So deep, with the healing gone. The reason I tell you that dream plan, is not only to highlight the power of lucid dreaming for trauma integration, but also to show that there's something in the dreamer that knows what you're ready for and what you're not. So aim high, and if it's too much, the dream will bring you back down. There's a famous Tibetan Lama called Tenzin Wangyal Rinpoche who wrote a book called "The Tibetan Yoga of Dream and Sleep." When I saw him teach in London, he said, "You Westerners, you love your plans. So write your plan, put it in your back pocket and go and live your life." That's the attitude we need to have to our dream plans. Don't feel too rigidly confined by them. It's just an exercise to help us manifest the cause and conditions to get lucid. And finally, if you do make a dream plan and you want to change it in the moment, that's totally fine too. If you've made a dream plan to go surfing for example, and then you're about to go surfing in the lucid dream and the Dalai Lama walks in and says, "I've come to give you the meaning of life." You don't say, "See you later, Dalai, I'm going surfing." You say, "Forget surfing, tell me the meaning of life." There are main ways that your dream plan will manifest. Number one, in a lucid dream. So maybe tonight, maybe in one week, maybe in one month. But whenever you have your next lucid dream, you become lucid, you call out your sankalpa, you engage the dream plan. The second way it can manifest, is through a non-lucid dream. Some people find the act of creating the dream plan, send such a powerful message to their dreamer saying, "I really want this to happen" that their dreamer engages the dream plan, even before they're lucid. So basically your dream plan plays out in a non-lucid dream. And the third way your dream plan can manifest, is quite spooky. In this waking life, sometimes creating the dream plan has such a powerful effect on the energetic environment, that a bit like creating a vision board, you might find that your dream plan manifests in the waking state before it's manifested in the dream state. First time I ever saw this, was it one of the retreats I was running at a place called Holy Isle. Now Holy Isle is a tiny Island, off a tiny Island, off the coast of Scotland, very little cell phone reception. While we're at that retreat, a lady made a dream plan that in the dream plan, she wanted to meet her estranged father. He wasn't dead, but they hadn't spoken for many months due to a big family bust up. And in the lucid dream she was going to have the conversation she wanted to have with him, and then engage a healing. Over the four days of that retreat, she didn't become lucid. But when we were leaving the retreat, getting the boat off the Island, and everyone's cell phone started beeping again. She went, "Oh my God, look at this text." There was a text message from her dad. After four months of not speaking, her dad had texted her saying, "This is ridiculous, it's been too long, let's meet up, I want to make things right again." Could it be that the universe had heard the call of the dream plan? So even though she didn't become lucid in her lucid life, the dream plan manifest. However your dream plan manifests, whenever it's complete, write a new one for your next lucid dream. Some of you might get lucid in this first week. Some of you might not get lucid until week three or four, either is totally, totally fine. But whenever you've completed the dream plan, make another. Now your actions for today. Make your dream plan and share it with the Tribe, asking for feedback if you want it, and offering feedback very sensitively if asked of you. Once you have a dream plan that you're really excited by, please do share it with the Tribe. Why share it? Because it adds to the power of manifestation. Your dream plan has been witnessed by another. It's been heard by the universe and all that will add power to the intent and encourage your dreamer to help you get lucid. Of course, keep up your dream recall, dream diaries and the Tribe dream sharing. So that's dream planning done dreamers but don't forget, your dream plan can and will evolve. So don't feel confined by it. And remember if it doesn't make you salivate, make another one that does. So until then, deep dreams.
