LESSON 16 – The Columbo Method for Noticing Reality
--------------------
<v ->Today, we learn how to be like Columbo.</v> Yup, I am talking about that same Columbo. Think back, if you can, to the 1970s TV homicide detective named Lieutenant Columbo. For those of you who didn't see the show, Columbo didn't have flashy forensics. I don't think he even had a gun, he just possessed a sharp mind that took in every detail of the environment while checking the facts and looking closely. Even the way they filmed Columbo helped to highlight this. So Lieutenant Columbo would walk into a room and then suddenly they'd cut the camera to POV, point of view, and the camera would scan around the room and then focus, as he realized, oh, there's something suspicious about this person that Columbo is recognizing. Then the camera would continue, and focus and you'd realize, ah, Columbo just spotted a clue. So even the way they filmed it highlighted this idea of looking closely, of searching for clues. And of course, that's just how we need to be if we want to solve the mystery of the lucid dream. We need to look closely for the clues, in our case, dream signs and dream anomalies. As I said yesterday, no reality check works 100% of the time and not every dream is full of obvious dream signs. So what can you do if your hand doesn't change but you're still sure that you must be dreaming. How can you spot a dream sign if the dreamscape is seemingly devoid of them? Well, you can bring out your notepad, scrunch up one eye and say, and one more thing. The Columbo method is fundamentally based upon creating a habit of looking closely in the waking state so that eventually we start to look closely in our dreams and thus, see more dream signs and dream anomalies and become lucid. It really works. First of all, let's see how to practice the Columbo method in a dream. If you think you might be dreaming, so you become pre-lucid, scan the areas for clues and touch things, really explore things. Remember, the lucid dream state looks amazingly realistic but if you look close enough, you'll often be able to see slight inconsistencies and realize that you're dreaming. It's a bit like watching a puppet show. If you've been watching the puppet show for long enough you stop seeing the strings and you just see the puppets, that's what we're like in our non-lucid dreams. What we need to do is to start looking closely and eventually if we look closely enough, we'll start to see the strings these little inconsistencies that the dreaming mind has forgotten to include. That's how we'll become lucid. Step two, in the dream, be mindful and ask yourself, how did I get here? What was I doing before? What's the last thing I can remember? This is a direct reference to the film "Inception". You might remember in that film, the first time one of our protagonists is taken into a lucid dream, they're sitting outside at a French cafe and the agent says to the lady, isn't it funny how in dreams, you don't travel places, you just seem to arrive? The lady replies, yeah, I guess so. He then says, how did we get to this cafe? And the woman goes, well, we... Ah, and she becomes lucid. When practicing the Columbo method in the daytime, every time your investigation leads to a new discovery, wow, I wasn't expecting to see that. Or, wow, the bark on that tree looks really weird. You know what to do? You do a reality check. So you see how the Columbo method combines with the weird technique. If you do this enough during the day, you will end up dreaming about doing it. And when you dream about it, you'll become lucid. By bringing more mindful awareness into everyday life, we become more mindfully aware in our dreams too. So anything we can do to boost our awareness while we live will help to boost our awareness while we dream. I want to offer you the opportunity to be guided through the process of doing the Columbo method during the daytime. So, we've created an audio track that you can listen to while you're practicing the Columbo method. The aim of this exercise is not only to look closely as a way of fostering a habit, which will flow into your dreams, but also it's the opportunity to see how many reality checks you can do within a short period of time. All you need is to find an area containing some interesting things within it. It might be outside in a park you haven't visited before, or it might be up in your attic, a place you haven't looked closely before. Find a space, listen to the audio and enjoy being detective Columbo. and now here are your actions for today. I want you to spend the day looking closely, just like Columbo, and then share your experience with the tribe. Try and do the Columbo method guided meditation at least once before you go to sleep tonight. Secondly, be on the lookout for more awareness of details within your dream tonight. It's very common that the first night after you practice the Columbo method, you dream full of details. And finally, keep up the three Ds and reality checks. The Columbo method is as much a lucid living technique as it is a lucid dreaming one. Ideally, we want our practice of the Colombo method to become a way of life, we want to become a person who looks deeply, who touches things, who notices details, who isn't duped by illusion, or fooled by reality. If we can learn to live lucidly, then we will soon start dreaming that way too. See you tomorrow. And until then, lucid dreams and a lucid life.

LESSON 16 – Meditation: The Columbo Method

The aim of this exercise is not only to look closely as a way of fostering a habit, which will flow into our dreams, but also it's the opportunity to see how many reality checks you can do in a short period of time. Find an area with some interesting things in it, maybe outside in a park, maybe a place you haven't visited before, maybe in your attic, anywhere that you haven't really explored closely before. I want you to walk around this area and become aware of your surroundings. Feel the ground beneath you. Become aware of your body as it moves through space. I want you to look closely at things, touch things, look behind things. Feel the texture of objects, and look closely at the detail. Notice colors, notice patterns, smell things, touch things. Look closely. And whenever you come across something that is unexpected, strange, or dreamlike, I want you to pause and to ask yourself, am I dreaming? And then answer that question by doing your chosen reality. Check. When your mind drifts off into chattering thoughts, or you feel yourself become distracted, bring your attention back to the task at hand. Looking closely. This is a six senses movement, meditation. Feel deeply look closely, become lucid in the dream. Every time you see something unexpected or weird, don't ignore it, acknowledge it, and ask yourself, could I be dreaming And answer the question with your chosen reality Check. But if not, feel free to bring your awareness back to normal consciousness. Keeping the flavor of the Columbo method within your body, but returning to fully conscious awareness.

LESSON 17 – The 4 Steps of Hypnagogic Affirmation
--------------------
<v ->Remember all that focus</v> that we put on the hypnagogic state last week? This is one of the techniques that we were prepping for. If ever there was a lucid dreaming technique that I had to choose above all others. It would be this one. This is the core lucid dreaming technique found in all the major lucid dream traditions. So it's got really good provenance. It's based on the hypnotic power of the hypnagogic state, plus the brain chemistry of our sleep cycle. It's called the Hypnagogic Affirmation Technique. Now I'm pretty sure you can imagine how this technique works. As you fall through the hypnagogic state you mentally recite a positive affirmation of your intent to gain lucidity. As we learned before, the hypnagogic state is very similar to the hypnotic state. So if we apply a suggestion or affirmation within it we may find it has the potential to work with hypnotic effect. Combine that with a direct entry, into dreaming that the hypnagogic state offers in the early hours and we have a very strong technique. Today, we'll learn the basic one. Tomorrow, we'll learn the Buddhist one, that's been refined over 1000 years of practice. The next day we'll learn the Mexican Toltec one which has been used by nagual and shamans in their lucid dreaming practice. And on the final day, we'll learn one that uses our dream plan Sankalpa to add power to the practice. The first thing to know is that this technique should best be practiced after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. You can do this technique as you first fall asleep at night. Nothing bad will happen, but I advise that when you first fall asleep tonight you continue the dream recall affirmation, and that you do this, the hypnagogic affirmation after awakening after at least four and a half to five hours of sleep. Why do we have to do this practice after waking up in the middle of the night? Remember, the first four and a half hours sleep is when you have most of your deep sleep periods. So we don't want to mess with that, otherwise we might be grumpy the next day. but the second four to five hours of sleep are when you have hypnagogic state that goes straight into long dream states. That's why we do the practice then. It gives us direct access from the hypnagogic into the dream. So yes, this practice may require you to set an alarm in the early hours of the morning. Or you can just use your bladder alarm if you usually wake up to pee, at least once a night. Anyone out there who's currently working with depression or anxiety though, just be aware that sometimes if we do a middle of the night wake up and our mind starts racing, it can be difficult to fall asleep again. So if you are currently working with depression or anxiety, maybe keep this particular technique on the back burner or at least take it easy for the time being. It might well be that it's perfectly fine and you fall asleep great, but just be aware that is a possibility. The important thing with this practice, is that you saturate your sleepy consciousness with the strong aspiration to have a lucid dream. When I was writing my first book, "Dreams of Awakening," I interviewed a female Tibetan Buddhist Lama named Lama Zangmo. In fact, she's the woman who runs the Buddhist center where I lived for seven years. In the interview, I asked her, "Lama, you've got over 11 years meditation retreat experience. If there was one tip that you could give people who are trying to learn to lucid dream, what would it be?" Now, I thought she was going to give me some esoteric Tibetan mantra or some hidden secret teaching, but actually, she said, "The one thing I'd say is you've got to be really fired up for it. If you're fired up for it, then lucidity will come." So again, it's all about enthusiasm and determination. So let's look at exactly how to practice the hypnagogic affirmation technique. Step one, after setting an alarm in the early hours, at least four and a half to five hours after you went to sleep, wake up and write down your dreams. Step two, put your dream diary away, turn off the light if it was on and enter back into the hypnagogic state. Step three, as you pass through the hypnagogic state, continuously recite in your own mind, the following affirmation, the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming, the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming, over and over in your mind as you pass through the hypnagogic. Recite the affirmation 21 times if you can. As we learned before, you can count 21 on your fingers, you can count 21 in your mind, or if the whole counting thing just seemed too neurotic, just recite the affirmation over and over for at least three or four minutes as you fall asleep. Try to recite the affirmation with real feeling and gusto. This is vital because without determination and enthusiasm, this technique simply won't work. Step four, once you've done your 21 recitations or recited the affirmation for at least a few minutes, allow yourself to fall asleep. So this technique shouldn't keep you awake for hours. You wake up, you write down your dreams. You enter back into the hypnagogic, reciting the affirmation, a few minutes later, you allow yourself to black out. The whole process is like 10 minutes max. The most important thing is not so much that you repeat the affirmation right up to the point where you enter the dream, although that would be great, but more that you saturate the last few minutes of your conscious awareness with that strong intention to gain lucidity aim for the affirmation, to be the last thing to pass through your mind before you black out. If you like, you can fall asleep tonight, guiding yourself through the hypnagogic affirmation technique. But if you prefer, I've recorded a guided audio meditation that you can listen to as you fall asleep, where I help you progress through the hypnagogic state, reciting the affirmation. So your actions for today, practice the hypnagogic affirmation technique tonight, after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. When you first fall asleep tonight, keep up the dream recall affirmation though, as well as the dream diaries, the Columbo mindset, the reality checks, all that good stuff. Tomorrow, we'll be learning about the Tibetan Buddhist tradition and the affirmation that they use. But for tonight, give this one a shot. Enjoy your lucid dreams.

LESSON 18 – The 4 Steps of Bodhichitta Hypnagogic Affirmation
--------------------
<v ->I formally became a Buddhist at the age of 19</v> and I lived in a Tibetan Buddhist center for over seven years. So I know something of the tradition and practices and I can safely say that one of the most powerful and most complete lucid dreaming traditions in the world is found in Tibet. Where the best of both Indian Buddhist techniques and those from indigenous Bon Shamanism combined to create a very powerful practice called Dream Yoga. In week four, we'll explore the four stages of the Buddhist lucid dream tradition called Dream Yoga or Milam Naljor, as it's known in Tibet. Milam means dream and Naljor is translated as yoga, but actually it means like the natural state. But for now, let me share some of the basics of this ancient dream tradition. The Dream Yoga teachings were first brought into Tibet in the eight century by Guru Padmasambhava a historical figure who pioneered Buddhism in Tibet. In fact, Guru Padmasambhava was also the guy who wrote the famous 'Tibetan book of the dead.' And the link between lucid dreaming and conscious dying is very strong, as we'll look at in depth later on in the Quest. When I first started hanging out at Buddhist centers, I used to hear the monks and nuns talk about this term Dream Yoga and I wasn't really sure what it was. One day I heard a monk saying, "Oh, it's our word for a series of practices used within Tibetan Buddhism that have lucid dream training at their core." When I heard this, I piped up and I said to the monk, "Oh, I can lucid dream." And the monk said, "Really, you can lucid dream? What do you do in your lucid dreams?" Now, at this point, I was just using lucid dreaming for sex and skateboarding, so I was a bit embarrassed, and I went "Just like skateboarding mainly." And he went, "Skateboarding?" I said, "Well, yeah, why do amongst use lucid dreaming anyway?" And he said "Oh, we use lucid dreaming to explore the nature of reality. We use lucid dreaming to prepare for the moment of our death. We use lucid dreaming to do our spiritual practice while we sleep." So let's break down those three main reasons to practice lucid dreaming within Tibetan Buddhism. The first reason is as a spiritual practice. Lucid dreaming itself is seen as a spiritual practice. Because every time you train the mind to see through illusion, as you do in a lucid dream, that same habit of mind will flow into the waking state allowing you to penetrate illusion in both your nighttime and your daytime experiences. But we can also use the lucid dream state to do our spiritual practice by going into the lucid dream. "Oh, wow, I'm dreaming", and then doing your spiritual practice in the lucid dream. So literally meditating within the lucid dream, or doing your Buddhist chanting within the lucid dream, or doing a certain tantric practice within the lucid dream. The second main reason for lucid dreaming within the Tibetan Buddhist tradition is preparation for death and dying. Now, we're going to look at this in depth later on in the Quest, but know this, it's believed that if you can train the mind to be lucid and aware in your dreams, then that same habit can have flow into the death and dying experience. So that when the dreamlike hallucinatory experiences of death and dying occur, we might be able to recognize them lucidly and go not "Aha, I'm dreaming", but "Aha, I'm dying." I know it sounds crazy, but it's believed that if you can die consciously, this is the highest achievement of the spiritual practitioner. And the third main reason for practicing lucid dreaming within the Tibetan Buddhist tradition is exploration of the nature of reality. Anyone who's had a lucid dream will know how amazingly realistic it seems. So the lucid dream proves to us how brilliant the human mind is at creating and sustaining illusion. So it's believed that if the human mind can do that in our dreams, maybe it's doing it in the waking state too. And maybe what we think is an objectively existing, dualistic separate reality, is, in fact, more like a dreamlike shared illusion that we are co-creating into existence. This isn't a Dallas moment where Buddhism says, "Oh, you're going to wake up and it's all been a dream", but it says what we think to be reality is far more dreamlike and co-created than we might believe it to be. Within the Tibetan Buddhist tradition it's believed that once you become lucid you have seven times the power of mind. So, in the lucid dream state, whatever you're doing in the lucid dream, your consciousness is 700% more powerful than it is in the waking state. Which means any healing you do, any prayers you say, any meditation you engage in a lucid dream is seven times more powerful than doing those things in the waking state. Now, isn't that interesting? Maybe that in one statement explains things like placebo healing in the lucid dream, explain things like trauma integration in the lucid dream, explain things like getting great insights about our career path or how we should live our lives. Perhaps, it's nothing so mystical than the brain removed from its limits that it has in the waking state and engaging its full 700% capacity. Until he died a few years ago, the greatest living dream Yogi was a man named Namkhai Norbu Rinpoche. And there's a famous quote from him where he says, "One moment of meditation within a lucid dream is equal to a one week meditation retreat in the waking state." Now, whenever I say that quote, I always think, how do we do the maths on that? You know, how many days in the week are we talking about, how many hours a day are we meditating? Who knows? But the point is, he's saying it's a very powerful place to do your spiritual practice. So whether you're a Buddhist, Christian, atheist or none of the above, doing a spiritual practice within the lucid dream is said to be a very beneficial thing to do. And if you're thinking, "I don't have a spiritual practice." Practice kindness. Kindness is the universal spiritual practice. Go into the lucid dream and send love to someone who needs it, or send love to yourself, that might be the person who needs it most. Nowadays, there's loads of technology available to help you have lucid dreams. There's apps that you can download onto your phone. There's Quests like this that you can do. There's even sleep masks that you wear as you fall asleep that flash red light through your eyelids bright enough to penetrate the dream, indicating to you that you're dreaming and giving you a lucid dream. Some of these work, some of them don't but it's interesting to know that the use of technology for lucid dreaming goes all the way back to medieval Tibet. There was a technique where two monks would buddy up. One of the monks would lie on the floor and fall asleep. The other monk would sit meditating, looking at the sleeping monk's face. And when the meditating monk saw the eyes of the sleeping monk flicking, rapid eye movement, they knew it meant that they were dreaming. So they would either whisper into the ear or through a bamboo stick, whisper down the end of it with the other end in the ear of the sleeping monk saying something like, "Know that you're dreaming, recognize the dream state, have a lucid dream." So maybe that's something you can practice tonight with your partner or whoever you're lucky enough to share the bed with. However, do make sure you have their consent before you start whispering in their ear. Especially, if you're going to stick a bamboo stick down there. The technique that we're about to learn for our hypnagogic affirmation is based on a concept called Bodhichitta. So we need to look at Bodhichitta before we begin. Bodhichitta is a Sanskrit word. Actually, it's made up of two words, Bodhi, which means the awakened mind and Chitta, which means heart, but not literally the heart, but more like the heart of the matter, like the essence of something. So we could roughly translate Bodhichitta as heart of the awakened mind or the essence of awakening. And Bodhichitta affirmation would be an affirmation to do something, not just for our own selfish reasons, but for the benefit of others. The entire Buddhist path is based upon Bodhichitta aspiration, doing something not just for ourselves, but for the benefit of others. Moving away from selfishness, connecting to others and knowing that the most important spiritual practice of all is kindness. The following technique should be practiced after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. As we discussed yesterday, after four and a half to five hours your hypnagogic starts to lead straight into REM dreaming. So it's best to it then. Even the medieval yogis knew this and they used the power of their intention to wake themselves up automatically after four or five hours. So you could try that too, if you like. Let's learn how to practice the Tibetan Buddhist Bodhichitta hypnagogic affirmation technique. Step one, just like yesterday's technique, after setting an alarm after at least four and a half to five hours sleep, wake up and write down your dreams. Step two, put your dream diary away, turn off the light if it was on and drop back into the hypnagogic state. Step three, once you're in the hypnagogic state, continuously recite the Bodhichitta affirmation over and over in your mind. The affirmation is, I lucid dream for the benefit of all beings. I lucid dream for the benefit of all beings. Recite that over and over in your mind as you fall asleep. Again, 21 times if you can or at least for a few minutes. Step four, fall asleep with that deep intention to lucid dream for the benefit of all beings. Remember Bodhichitta is about moving out of selfishness and doing stuff based on kindness for others. Again, just like the previous technique you could choose to guide yourself through the Bodhichitta affirmation by yourself tonight, alone. But if you prefer, I've recorded a guided audio meditation that you can listen to as you fall asleep that will help guide you through the hypnagogic, reciting this Bodhichitta affirmation. And now your actions for today. Firstly, practice the Bodhichitta affirmation technique tonight after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. Remember, when you first fall asleep, good to do the dream recall affirmation, then practice this one after at least four and a half to five hours. And as always, keep up the dream diary, keep up the reality checks, keep up the Columbo mindset. So far we've got the basic lucid dream affirmation, that's good for everyone. We've got the Buddhist one, that might be better for the spiritual types and tomorrow we're going to learn the Mexican Toltec affirmation, which is great for the shamanic warriors among you. But until then, I wish you Bodhichitta dreams.

LESSON 19 – The 4 Steps of Toltec Hypnagogic Affirmation
--------------------
<v ->In 2014, I met a guy named Sergio Magana,</v> at a dream conference that we were both speaking at. He was described to me as the new Carlos Castaneda. So naturally, I gravitated towards him, we became friends, and in fact, we soon began teaching together, running joint workshops, exploring the similarities between my tradition, Tibetan Buddhism, and his tradition Toltec Mexihca shamanism. Other than Buddhism this is the tradition in which I've received the most training and the lucid dream practices within the Toltec tradition are as powerful as they are ancient. Let's learn one together and step onto the path of the dream warrior. Before we learn this technique, it's important to know something about the history of the tradition. So first of all, let's look at Toltec. The Toltec were a group of shamanic practitioners that existed hundreds of years ago in ancient Mexico. Toll means measure and the Toltec were the people of the measure. They use astrological measures applied to breathe practices as a way to change consciousness. Mexihca means people of the navel of the moon. And in fact, a Mexi-can is not somebody who necessarily lives in Mexico. It was a practitioner of the shamanic practices of the Mexihca. Because interestingly when the Spanish invaded Mexico was one place name they didn't change. So Mexihca are people of the navel of the moon. Within the Toltec Mexihca there are five core practices. One of them is breathwork. It's like a former Mexican pranayama breathing through certain nostrils at certain rates to have an effect on consciousness. The second is the use of masks and mirrors to disidentify our sense of self. The third one is dance using certain movements of the body and musical rhythms to have an effect on consciousness. The fourth is plant medicine that has a psychophysical effect on the body and the mind. And the fifth practice is lucid dreaming. When I first met Sergio I started doing some of his practices. Some of them include masks and mirrors, where you stand in front of a mirror wearing the mask of certain animals. Not only as a way to help you disidentify with your sense of self, but also as a way to bring these animal archetypes into your lucid dreams. The first time I knew this had really worked was when I was in a lucid dream and I suddenly realized my body felt different, I'd transformed. I looked up and I saw a horn. I looked down and I saw these little trotters and I realized I transformed into a unicorn. I mean, when I was doing the mask and mirror work I wasn't using a unicorn mask. But I can only assume that my dreaming mind had gone, "Look, I'm going to transform you into an animal, Charlie and the most fabulous animal I can think of is a fricking unicorn." The following affirmation technique makes use of the term dream warrior. Just like yesterday's technique used the term bodhicitta. We need to make sure we know what we mean by dream warrior before we begin. Some of you might be triggered by this thinking dream warrior, isn't that about violence and fighting? No, within the Toltec Mexihca tradition, lucid dreaming is said to be the path of the warrior. Not because the lucid dream encourages us to fight aspects of our shadow or unconscious, but because it takes the discipline and courage of a warrior to have a lucid dream. Think about it. Think about the discipline I'm asking you to have. To wake up at five o'clock in the morning to write down your dreams. To go around all day, checking your hands, being aware of reality. To write down your dreams. This is a disciplined practice. You want to have a lucid dream. You need to have the discipline of a warrior. And secondly, this is a courageous practice. It said it takes the courage of a warrior to know that whatever you see in the lucid dream is you. When we start meeting angels and spiritual figures in your lucid dreams, it's very easy for us to go, these are my inner angels, these are my inner spiritual figures. But when we start meeting our inner monsters, our inner demons, it takes great courage to say, "Yes, that's me too." And not to slip into a dualistic view of, "Oh, it's something from the collective unconscious," or, "Oh, it's a dark Shaman entering my mind." So this is why we are dream warriors. It takes the discipline of a warrior to have a lucid dream and the courage to accept that whatever we meet in the lucid dream is us. And that's okay. So again, the following technique should be practiced after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. Now the Mexicans, rather than using an alarm clock they used to use something else to wake them up after four hours sleep. They would use fig wine. Now fig wine is a diuretic. So if you drink some before bed you'll going to need to pee a few hours later. So what they used to do is before a lucid dreaming session, they'd pour fig wine into a kind of a small shot glass. And they'd offer one to the moon that offer one to the earth and then you could take one shot yourself. You could take a second, but if you took three shots that were disrespectful. And then after one or two shots of fig wine, you'd fall asleep and naturally about four or five hours later your bladder would wake you up. So if you don't fancy an alarm clock tonight and you can get your hands on some Mexican fig wine you could always try that. So here's how to practice the Toltec Mexihca dream warrior affirmation technique. Step one, just like yesterday's technique after setting an alarm or using your fig wine, wake up after at least four and a half to five hours sleep and write down your dreams. Step two, put your dream diary away, turn off the light if it was on and enter the hypnagogic. Step three, once in the hypnagogic state continuously recite the dream warrior affirmation over and over again in your mind. The affirmation is this. I am a warrior of the dream state. I stay lucid and conscious while dreaming. I am a warrior of the dream state. I stay lucid and conscious while dreaming. Again recite the affirmation 21 times if you can, with real feeling and gusto like a warrior. This is vital because without that warrior mindset, again the technique simply won't work. And step four, fall asleep, ready to get lucid. Again, just like the last two versions of this technique. If you like, you can fall asleep, reciting the affirmations by yourself. But if you'll prefer, I've recorded a guided audio meditation that you can listen to as you fall asleep. Where I'm helping you, guiding you through the affirmation and through the hypnagogic. And now your actions for today. Firstly, practice the Toltec warrior affirmation tonight, after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. LESSON 20 – The Multiple Wake-Ups Technique for Lucid Dreaming
--------------------
<v ->Both the Buddhist</v> and the shamanic traditions hold a lot of power. And so using their affirmations, helps us to tap in the power of those lineages. However, the power of your own intention and the power of our of why we want to have a lucid dream can be just as effective as any ancient lineage. In this technique, we use the essence of our dream plan as the affirmation itself. So it's not only a great way to embed the dream plan into our unconscious memory, but it also encourages the dream plan to manifest quicker too. As with the previous techniques, this one should be practiced after at least four and a half to five hours sleep. The Buddhists use the power of the human mind. The Mexicans use the power of fig wine and we, as modern humans, can use the power of the alarm clock. So however you want to wake up, just make sure you wake up in the second half of the night. So let's look at how to practice the dream plan affirmation technique. Step one, just like yesterday's technique, after setting an alarm and the early hours, wake up and write down your dreams. Step two, dream diary away, light off if it was on, enter hypnagogic again. Step three, once in the hypnagogic state, continuously recite an affirmation that combines the first one we learnt, the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming, with the essence of your dream plan or San kalpa. For example, the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming when I know that I'm dreaming I, and then insert the essence of your dream plan or San kalpa, such as, meet my inner child or the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming. When I know that I'm dreaming I heal my elbow or the next time I'm dreaming, I know that I'm dreaming. When I know that I'm dreaming, I ask for spiritual advice. Whatever your dream plan is, you add in the essence of that dream plan, or perhaps the San kalpa itself, to the affirmation. As always, you recite at 21 times if you can, or at least for a few minutes, as you fall off to sleep. Step four, fall asleep mindfully, with a deep intention to have a lucid dream and to engage your dream plan. As with the previous three versions of this technique, if you want, you can go into the hypnagogic by yourself tonight, but if you'd prefer, I've recorded a guided audio meditation to help guide you through the process and into the hypnogogic. Now that we've learnt four different variations of the hypnagogic affirmation technique, I want to share with you another technique that will give you four different chances to practice them. It's called multiple wake-ups. Within both the Tibetan Buddhist and Mexican dream traditions, the yogis and Sharman soon discovered that if you only go to bed once and wake up once in the morning, you only have one chance to get lucid, but you wake multiple times, then you have multiple opportunities. Remember how our sleep cycle works. The first three cycles, so the first four and a half hours, are mainly deep sleep with short bits of dreaming but after four and a half hours, it flips. And we start to have long dream periods and our entry into the dream periods is much quicker. So, the multiple wake-ups technique harnesses this power of the dream cycle. Essentially, you allow yourself to sleep for at least four and a half to five hours, and then you wake up, fall asleep for a 90 minute cycle, before waking up again and falling asleep for another 90 minute cycle, however many times you want. On the lucid dreaming immersive retreats that I run, this multiple wake-ups technique is one that really separates the group. Some people love it. They see it gives them multiple chances to have lucid dreams. Other people hate it 'cause they don't want to be woken up in the middle of the night by me. On the lucid dreaming retreats, people spend the first half of the night sleeping alone in their rooms. Let's say from 10:30 to 3:30, they're just sleeping. However, at 3:30, they wake up and they move into the sacred sleeping area where they have bed number two set up, and then they hand themselves over to me as their human alarm clock. I guide them through the hypnagogic state, back in the dream. And then 90 minutes later, I wake them up again with the sound of Tibetan bells. Then, they spend five minutes writing down their dreams and then I guide them back into the hypnagogic, back into sleep. Then 90 minutes later, again I wake them. They write down their dreams, back asleep. 90 minutes later, I wake them again and then it's breakfast time. This gives us not one session of lucid dreaming but four sessions a night, quadrupling your chances of engaging the practice and having a lucid dream. Now of course, we're not on one of my lucid dreaming retreats, but if you want, you can still try this technique yourself. Give yourself at least four and a half to five hours sleep. Then wake up, write down your dreams reset your alarm for 90 minutes later and fall back asleep during the lucid dreaming technique. Then 90 minutes later, the alarm goes off. You wake up, you write down your dreams. You reset the alarm for another 90 minute session and you fall asleep during the lucid dreaming technique. You can do this once a night, twice a night, or even have the full four sessions that we have on our lucid dreaming retreat. See what works for you. The timing of your schedule is totally up to you. On the retreats, we usually do the first session, 10:30 PM to 3:30 AM. The second session, 3:30 AM to 5:00 AM. The third session, 5:00 AM to 6:30 AM and then the final session, 6:30 to 8:00 AM. Of course, you can work out your own schedule. It's totally up to you. Now I'm not you practice this technique every night of the week. That's going to mess with your sleep cycle, so don't do it. But maybe once a week, twice a week, you can think of it like a lucid dreaming date night, a night where you really spend time with you and your dreamer. Remember though, if you're currently working with depression, anxiety or racing thoughts, this technique might not be the one for you. Feel free to give it a go, but take it easy. And now our actions for today. First of all, practice the dream plan affirmation technique at least once tonight, after at least four and a half to five hours sleep, you get it. Secondly, practice if you like, 'cause it's totally optional, the multiple wake-ups technique. Maybe you could do two wake-ups, three, whatever you like but however many you do, every time you fall asleep, be sure to try out your favorite hypnagogic affirmation technique, any of the four we've learnt. Be sure to keep up the three D's, dream diaries, dream recall, dream signs, be doing your reality checks during the day and keep up that Colombo mindset. Also, I'd like you to check in with the tribe and share, how is your third week going? Have you had any lucid dreams yet? Have you seen your hand change in your dreams? Have you had any interesting Colombo moments? Let the tribe know. Okay, so we've now got four variations of the hypnagogic affirmation technique down. You can either practice all of them or you can just choose your favorite and focus on that one, it's totally up to you. Some of you might find you really resonate with the warrior affirmation. Others of you might not and you think, nah, I like the dream plan one. Whichever one's your favorite, feel free to focus on that. The main thing is that you do the hypnagogic affirmation technique regularly, every night if you can, or at least three times a week from now on. And finally, well done, you completed week three, you are truly a warrior of the dream state and like all good warriors, you need your rest. So tomorrow is our day off. Well kind of, apart from the weekly quiz, the pep talk and of course everybody's favorite, the bedtime story, until then, sweet dreams.

So let's do a lowdown on the technique so far. Dream recall. The dream recall affirmation can be done every single night and writing in the dream diary can be done every single morning. That's standard. As for spotting dream signs, eventually being aware of your dream signs will form part of the process of writing down your dreams. As each time you write into your dream diary, you start to notice the dream signs as you write them. As for reality checks, your use of the weird technique needs to become so frequent that your friends or family need to start asking you, "Dude, what's wrong with your hands?" If they haven't started asking you what's wrong with your hands, you probably haven't been doing it enough. So make sure you're constantly doing the weird technique. And finally, the hypnagogic affirmation technique, whichever of the four variations you choose, this can be practiced as much as you like after at least four and a half hours of sleep and at least three times a week, if possible. If you're currently managing to do all this, then you are hitting the ball out the park and if not, that's cool too. Take your time and celebrate whatever progress you are making. Now I know that some of you have already been having lucid dreams. So needless to say, once you've completed your current dream plan, just go back and write another. And for those of you who are yet to become lucid, no worries at all, this is a cumulative process and it can take quite a while. It's a deep process and it's often those with the deepest minds who take the longest time to get lucid. Maybe you haven't had any lucidity yet, but maybe you've been able to decode a really powerful dream. Maybe you haven't seen your hand change in your dream yet, but you have had a deep lucid living moment while practicing the Colombo method. All of this is great. Celebrate whatever successes you've been having. And remember, even if you manage to have a lucid dream every night of your life, 80% of your dream experience will still be non-lucid. If you ask me my top five dream experiences of all time. Okay, number one is a lucid dream, is that one where I visited Dewachen in the Varnek realm, but number two, three and four are actually non-lucid dreams. They were dreams where the message the dream was giving me was so powerful, so deep, so profound. It almost didn't want me to become lucid. It was as if the dream was like, now Charlie, just sit over that, don't fly. Don't call out Sankalpa, just witness what we're showing you. So it's not only about lucid dreams. It's about connecting to the dreamer with lucid awareness. So now your actions for today. Firstly, catch up on any content that you've missed. Secondly, keep up the three D's and reality checks as per normal, but tonight maybe give yourself a night off from lucid dreaming and fall asleep with the relaxation of my bedtime story. Until then, sweet dreams.

LESSON 21 – Meditation: Bedtime Story: “Vajra Killaya, Vajra Killoya”

Get into bed, get comfy, and allow yourself to drift off to sleep. While I tell you a bedtime story, I want to tell you a story about a man, a mountain, and a mantra. There was once a meditating yogi living in a cave up a mountain on an island in the middle of a lake. This meditating yogi had become famous throughout the whole of Tibet, and one day a young monk decided to go and visit him. The young monk got into a boat and rode across the lake to the island. He walked up the mountain on the island and up to the cave, and in the cave he found the meditating yogi reciting over and over again, the mantra of his chosen deity. Vara Kalay. The monk appeared into the cave and saw the yogi practicing. After a few minutes, the monk realized something. The famous yogi was mispronouncing the mantra of vara. He heard the yogi reciting it, saying On vara hung, on vara hung. But the actual mantra should be said on vara hung. The young monk couldn't just stand there and watch this happening. He had to say something so quietly he entered the cave and tapped the meditating yogi on the shoulder. The yogi turned round and looked at the monk with Surprise, yes, my son, how can I help you? The yogi said, to which the young monk replied, master, with all due respect, I think you've been making a mistake. The mantra of vara is pronounced on vara. Hung not on. Vara lawyer hung, really, the meditating yogi replied, I've been saying it wrong my whole life. For 30 years I've been sitting here meditating on this deity. Are you telling me this whole time I've been saying the mantra incorrectly? Well, yes, I'm sorry, master, but that does seem to be the case. And with that, the young monk left the cave, walked down the mountain onto the island, got into his boat, and rode across the lake. When he was about halfway across the lake, he heard something. It was the voice of the meditating yogi. The young monks spun round, and as he did so there was the yogi walking across the surface of the water, walking up to his boat. The yogi said to him, excuse me, my son, but how do you pronounce the mantra again? With that, the young monk was amazed and realized that the power of the mantra was found. Not in the pronunciation, but in the devotion of its recitation. Just as the story of the monk, the yogi, and the vaai mantra shows us the power of this practice is found in the devotion of intention, not in whether we pronounce it the right way. So however you are doing your lucid dreaming practice, that's fine as long as you're doing it from your heart. Don't get too caught up in the ifs or buts of each technique. Just make sure you are practicing from your heart. That's how you'll have lucid dreams by acting from the energy of your heart. And now with the energy of that story in your body, close your eyes if they're still open. Allow yourself to release into the support of your bed and relax deeply into sleep. Allow your breathing to slow and your mind to relax deeply into sleep. Allow any tension in your body to dissolve as you exhale and relax deeply into sleep. Dream lucidly and fruitfully. Relax deeply into sleep.

Week 4: Advanced Techniques for Lucid Dreaming

====================

LESSON 22 – Overcoming Lucid Nightmares
--------------------
<v ->Welcome to week four</v> where we learn how to harness the power of our shadow, explore the more advanced practices of Tibetan Dream Yoga and learn a lucid dreaming technique that boosts your chances of lucidity by up to 2000%. This week, we'll take you to the edge of your comfort zone with lessons on lucid dying, embracing your demons in the lucid dream, and even a group psychic experiment, in which I invite you guys to dive deep inside my mind through lucid dreaming. Any course on dreaming would be incomplete without a session on nightmares, but especially a course about lucid dreaming. Why? Because for lucid dreamers nightmares are opportunities. They can be embraced, transformed, and used as techniques for becoming lucid. Studies have shown that over a third of all spontaneous lucid dreams begin as nightmares. So nightmares can be really good news for lucid dreamers, but how does this actually work? Have you ever been in a nightmare and thought to yourself, "I've got to wake up, I've got to wake up." If so that nightmare was a lucid dream because the moment you acknowledged there was a place to wake up to, you had indirectly acknowledged that you were dreaming. Every time you wake up from a nightmare, however, the psychological trauma or psychological energy that led to the creation of the nightmare remains unintegrated. This is why our nightmares recur so much more than our seemingly happy dreams. That dream while you're having a dinner date, with your favorite celebrity, why did you only have it once? Because you love the dream as you were having it. And it was fully integrated in the moment. But that nightmare, that bad dream, that scary dream about the thing that happened to you or the thing you'd rather not think of, why does that dream come back week after week or month after month? Because the energy of the nightmare remains unintegrated due to us pushing it away and waking up from it. But why should nightmare so often lead to lucidity? Well, imagine this, if I somehow managed to travel through the screen right now and jump into your lap, you'll probably be so scared that your eyes would widen, your sense will become more alert, and you'll definitely become more aware of the situation, right? And also I hope you'll do a reality check, just to be sure. So research has shown us that the boost of awareness that fear brings is an evolutionary trait that helps us deal with potential threat. That same process happens when we feel scared or threatened in our dreams. Our awareness is boosted and boost our awareness in a dream and that can often lead to fully conscious lucidity. For some people, chronic nightmares are a serious complaint that not only affects the quality of their sleep, but also the quality of their lives. The good news though, is that if you can experience a nightmare with full lucidity, you have a powerful opportunity for trauma resolution and shadow integration. More about that tomorrow. I've taught lucid dreaming to loads of people suffering from post-traumatic stress disorder, including ex-soldiers, victims of terrorist attacks, and even people who've experienced abuse during childhood. And I've seen firsthand just how powerful lucid dream training can be. Not only in curing nightmares, but possibly more importantly in opening people up to a new perspective on sleep and dreams, in which they see their nightmares as a call for help, rather than an attack from the unconscious. Tomorrow, we'll learn exactly what to do if you ever get lucid within a nightmare. But for now, I want to share with you a beautiful guided meditation that I created inspired from a Tibetan shamanic technique for people who are currently having nightmares like the veterans with PTSD, but not yet having lucid dreams. Having said that, this is one of my most popular meditations among lucid dreamers too. And it's a great practice for anyone who wants to fall asleep with more confidence and peace. This is a visualization practice in which we imagine a gathering of protectors around us fearlessly supporting our shadow work, standing over us while we sleep. This practice is inspired by the Tibetan Bon Dream Yoga teachings, in which it said that if you're feeling anxious before sleep you should try to turn your sleeping area into a sacred, protected space by imagining that you're surrounded by enlightened beings and Dakinis. Dakini is a Sanskrit word that means sky dancer, and it usually refers to female embodiments of enlightenment. It said that as you fall asleep, imagine that these Dakinis remain like mothers watching over a child or guardian surrounding a king or queen. It's such a lovely way to fall asleep, surrounded by power, protection, and enlightened energy. So you might be thinking, who do I imagine as my allies and protectors around the bed? Anyone or anything who offers you a personal feeling of love, safety, allegiance, and protection. Your protectors can be living or dead, real or unreal, known or unknown, but make sure that they hold a personal place in your heart and give you a feeling of protection and power. The Circle of Protectors exercise is best done as you're first falling asleep at night. So I've created a guided audio meditation that you can listen to as you're falling asleep tonight. It takes you step-by-step through the exercise and is a great practice for anyone, who wants to fall asleep with a sense of peace and protection. and now it's time for your actions for today. Number one, practice the Circle of Protectors meditation as you fall asleep. Secondly, practice one of the Hypnagogic Affirmation techniques tonight after at least four and a half hours of sleep. And of course keep up the Dream Recall affirmation when you first fall asleep, the Dream Diaries all the time, Reality Checks all the time, and keep up that Columbo mindset. The Circle of Protectors is one of the most powerful practices for reducing nightmare frequency. As it allows us to fall asleep, feeling empowered, and trauma of course, is a disempowering experience. Tomorrow, we'll learn something just as empowering, how to increase our chances of becoming lucid by up to 2000%? As well as my flagship technique, Lucid Dream Shadow Work. Until then, sweet dreams and sweet nightmares.

====================

LESSON 22 – Meditation: Circle Of Protectors

Take a moment to make yourself totally comfortable lying down on the floor or on your bed if you like. And now take a moment to consider the protectors that you are going to be working with today. Your protectors can be alive or dead, known or unknown, real or unreal, but whoever or whatever they are needs to create a feeling of love and protection within you. Close your eyes and relax into the support of the bed with the ground beneath you. Come into an awareness of your breathing, noticing how your body expands a little with the inbreath and how your body releases and relaxes on the outbreath. I want you to imagine something. Imagine that your first protector is standing or sitting near your head. Feel the energy of their love and protection as they look down upon you lovingly. It doesn't matter if you can't see them clearly. Just know that they're there and keeping that visualization of your first protector by your head. Now, I want you to imagine your second protector sitting or standing by your feet. Your second protector is sitting or standing by your feet. Feel the energy of their love and protection. Connect to their power. It doesn't matter if you can't see them clearly. Just know that they're there. And now to your left hand side, I want you to imagine your third protector. Your third protector is sitting or standing by your left hand side. Feel their energy. Connect to the power of their love. Again, it doesn't matter if you can't see them clearly. Just know that they're there, feel that they're there. And now I want you to imagine that your fourth protector is sitting or standing on your right hand side. Connect with the energy of your fourth protector. If you can't see them clearly, just know that they're there. Connect deeply to the power of love and protection coming from your fourth protector on your right hand side. And now that your primary four protectors are positioned in all the four cardinal directions, north, south, east, and west. And now I invite you to fill in the gaps between your four primary protectors. Fill in the gaps with anything else that brings you a sense of love and protection. This could be people, places, animals, energies, star constellations, planets, gods, or deities. Whatever brings you a sense of love and protection, use them to fill in the gaps between your four primary protectors. You are now encircled by protection. You are now encircled by love. Take a moment to feel deeply into the love and protection that surround you. You are surrounded by love. You are surrounded by protection. And with this feeling of love and protection encircling, you either move on to your chosen, lucid dreaming technique, or allow yourself to slip off into sleep with this energy of love, power, and protection, permeating your entire night's sleep.

LESSON 23 – Shadow Work in Lucid Dreaming
--------------------
<v ->Today we learn about one of the most powerful sources</v> of energy in the psychophysical system, the energy that fuels the fire of awakening, this energy is called the shadow. In 2017, I wrote a book about the shadow called dreaming through darkness which shows people how to transform the power of their shadow side through a series of powerful meditations, esoteric exercises and of course dreamwork. Shadow work gives us more energy, more authenticity and a greater capacity for spiritual growth as we transform that which we hide from others into the power of awakening. One of the most effective ways to transform the shadow is to meet, befriend and embrace it within our lucid dreams but before that, let's make sure we know exactly what we're working with. So what is the shadow? There is a source of energy within us that contains the seed of awakening. We may hide it from others though we know that it's there, like fire, if ignored or misused it may burn us but if harnessed, it can warm us, protect us and revolutionize our life. We call this energy the shadow. The concept of the shadow is found within almost every culture and spiritual tradition the world over but it was first popularized in the West by the legendary Swiss psychiatrist, Carl Jung. He used the term to describe the part of the unconscious mind made up of all the seemingly undesirable aspects of the psyche. The shadow is our dark side but not dark meaning negative or malign, rather dark meaning yet to be illuminated. The shadow is comprised of everything within us that we don't want to face, that is everything both seemingly harmful and potentially enlightening. All that we have rejected, denied, disowned or repressed forms the shadow. So the shadow is not evil or bad, it's simply the parts of ourselves that seem incompatible with who we think we are. These might include our shame, our fears, our emotional wounds but also and crucially, our awakened essence, our unexpressed talents, our highest potential. Psychologists might define the shadow as all that we deem unacceptable and deny within ourselves. I've heard Shamans say the shadow resides anywhere that your fear becomes greater than your current capacity for love and I've heard Buddhists say that all of the aspects of ourselves that we don't want to face, that's the shadow. Personally, I define the shadow as anything within us that we are currently unwilling to accept or extend our love to. Many people when they first hear about the shadow, they immediately think about the potentially harmful traits like anger, prejudice, hatred, sexual taboos, what might be considered unacceptable. Interestingly though, the shadow contains just as many if not more overtly beneficial traits such as inner strength, blinding beauty and talents that we have kept hidden. So the shadow has two sides, dark and golden. The dark shadow contains the rejected traits that we deemed to be negative or harmful such as anger, fear, shame. It contains the answers to questions like what am I most afraid of? What am I most ashamed of? What parts of myself do I hide from myself and others? Never forget though, the dark shadow is neither bad nor harmful, it is simply unintegrated energy and until we learn how to tap into that energy and transmute its power, we will never become a fully integrated or fully awakened human being. The poet Rainer Maria Rilke once said, perhaps everything terrible is in its deepest essence something helpless that wants our love and so it is even with the darkest depths of our shadow. Becoming a shadow worker as you will see is all about love. Now to the golden shadow, the golden shadow often called the positive shadow is made up of our hidden talents, our blinding beauty, our unfulfilled potential, it's still made up of aspects of ourself that we deny, reject or disown but the more overtly beneficial aspects than found in the dark shadow. The golden shadow contains our intuition, our creativity, our childlike vitality and our spiritual power. Just as the dark shadow is made up of all the parts of ourselves that we fear may lead to rejection. the golden shadow is made up of all the bright, brilliant and magnificent parts of ourselves that we fear maybe too great, too awesome and too challenging to reveal to ourselves and others. For many people, it's their spiritual side that forms the core of their golden shadow so I ask you, anyone watching this quest, do any of you hide your esoteric or spiritual side from your friends or family for fear of being labeled too woo-woo, yeah? That's your golden shadow, it's your spiritual side, your esoteric side, what could be more powerful and beneficial and yet we hide it? Why? Same reason we hide a dark shadow. We're scared that if we reveal ourselves fully, we'll be rejected and the fear of rejection is a powerful fear. So the golden shadow is our unactivated potential, our unused talents and just as it takes conscious effort to reclaim and transform the energy stored in our dark shadow, so too must we make an effort to reclaim the golden shadow's energy if we want to be complete, balanced and authentically whole. It is possible to meet the actual personification of your shadow within your lucid dreams. In fact anyone who's ever had a lucid nightmare will probably already have done this, because nightmares are the realm of the dark shadow. However, we can if we feel ready actually call forth the shadow into our lucid dreams whether they're nightmarish or not. If we can do that and crucially if we can embrace whatever appears with love, then we engage one of the most transformative lucid dreaming techniques there is, lucid dream shadow work. So let's look at exactly how to practice this technique. Step one, if you're lucky enough to become a lucidly aware within a nightmare, try to stay in the nightmare for as long as you can, reminding yourself that it's all just a projection of your own mind and that nothing in the nightmare can do you harm. As Krishna Mercy once said, the great Indian philosopher, the seeing is the doing. Simply by witnessing the nightmare with lucid awareness, the trauma that led to the creation of the nightmare will often be spontaneously integrated. Many people find that by shifting their perspective like this, they can actually create a total transformation of attitude that tells the nightmare, I see you, I witness you, I understand that you are an expression of my own mind that just wants to be seen and once the nightmare has received this message of acceptance, it often dissolve spontaneously and never return, why? Because as we learned earlier, if we can look at the nightmare without judgment and knowing that it's just an unintegrated expression of our own shadow rather than some sort of externalized demon, it's energy will be naturally and fully integrated. Step two, this step requires the courage of the dream warrior. Essentially, once you become lucid in any dream nightmarish or not, proactively seek out and embrace your shadow, yup, literally hug it. Now let me explain all this hugging things in your lucid dream. As I mentioned before if you want to fly through the sky in a lucid dream, you might put your hand up like Superman and fly through the sky. You've given the dream the symbol of flight so what could be more symbolic of love, acceptance and integration than the hug? In fact, I often say if you're in a lucid dream and you can't remember your dream plan and you think what should I spend the lucid dream doing, just run around and hug everything. Hug the people, hug the trees, definitely hug the monsters and the demons because everything in the lucid dream is you and if there's something particularly scary, something that's monstrous, something you don't want to go near, that's most likely simply a part of your shadow that has become split off and the way to integrate it, embrace it with love. You might be thinking, how will know what's my shadow and what's not my shadow? Basically, anything in the dream that makes you feel scared all in all, that's your shadow. Your dark shadow might appear as a scary dream character whereas your golden shadow might appear as an awesome ball of light, an angel that blinds the eyes to look at it. Whether it's dark or golden, as soon as you see a shadow aspect in your lucid dream go forth and hug it, show it your love and integrate into the wholeness of your own psychology. Step three, there is a third option and this one is only for those who are really ready for radical change. Essentially, rather than waiting for the nightmarish shadow aspects to come to you, you become lucid and intentionally call forth the shadow. How does this work? You become lucid oh right, I'm dreaming and then within the lucid dream you call out something like shadow, come to me. Now I should say, if you are going to go into a lucid dream and call out shadow come to me, you better put your seatbelt on because you've just asked for the sum total of all your hidden traumas, shame, fear and divine power to appear in personified form. Once you call out for your shadow, whatever appears might have a very powerful effect on your psychophysical system so whatever it is you know the score, meet it, greet it and embrace it with love, fearlessly knowing that whatever it is, it's part of you and that to embrace it with love is the most powerful integration there is. If you can do that just once in a lucid dream, then it could be that as one jungian psychotherapist on one of my retreats once told me, you can integrate as much shadow material in one lucid dream as in six years of therapy, his words not mine. Meeting and embracing your shadow in a lucid dream is undoubtedly one of the most powerful forms of shadow integration but we needn't wait until we get lucid to start making friends with our shadow. The power of imagination is a great way to begin so I've created a guided audio meditation that you can listen to day or night, that asks you to imagine meeting, befriending and transforming your shadow, enjoy. And now your actions for today. Firstly, practice the meeting your shadow guided meditation that I just mentioned, secondly try and meet your shadow in your next lucid dream if you can or at least in the one after the lucid dream in which you complete your current dream plan. Of course practice one of the hypnagogic affirmation techniques tonight after at least four and a half hours sleep and be sure to keep up the dream diaries, spotting your dream signs, reality checks, the weird technique and Colombo mindset. As can see our toolbox is really filling up now and don't forget to check in with your tribe, keep up those dream circles, share your experience with the other people on the quest. Lucid dream shadow work is one of my all time favorite practices and I've been encouraging people to try it ever since my 2011 TED Talk on the subject, so please do try it. I know it can seem a bit scary though so just at some point whenever you're ready and don't forget, you could always get lucid and call out to meet your golden shadow rather than your dark shadow. However, I found meeting my inner light can be just as mind blowing and sometimes just as scary as meeting my inner darkness. Tomorrow, we'll learn about the four stages of Tibetan dream yoga and see how embracing the shadow and facing your fears in a lucid dream is actually an ancient practice that predates Carl Jung by about 900 years. Until then sweet dreams, sweet nightmares and enjoy your shadow.
