LESSON 24 – The Four Stages of Dream Yoga
--------------------
<v ->Remember back in week three,</v> when we touched on Tibetan and dream yoga. In today's lesson, we're going to go deep into the four stages of dream yoga, and discover the dream plans of the yogis of ancient Tibet. The Buddhist dream yoga tradition is without doubt, the most comprehensive in the world, and has been so for hundreds of years. So their techniques can be counted on. Now, let's explore the four stages of dream yoga. The four stages are, recognition, transformation, multiplication and unification. Don't worry, we're going to break down each one right now. The first stage, recognition. Essentially, this stage is about recognizing the fact that you're dreaming. It's about learning to lucid dream basically. When I spoke to my Buddhist master, Lama Meshi Rimbushje about this, he said recognition stage is about training the mind to know that you're dreaming as you're dreaming, use any techniques that work best for you to do so. As in use the Buddhist techniques, use the Mexican techniques, use the Western techniques. It doesn't matter so much what techniques you use just learn to lucid dream, that's stage one recognition. The second stage of dream yoga is called transformation. Now you might think they're talking about transforming the dreamscape there but actually, they're talking about transforming fear. So straight off the bat, you see that even in ancient Tibet, they believe most beneficial thing to do once you become lucid, is to work with your shadow to transform your fear. If we look at the translations of the Tibetan teachings, they say things like, once you have recognized the dream, walk into fire, knowing that you cannot be burnt, jump from a high place, knowing that you cannot fall. And my favorite, call forth a tiger into the dream. Once the tiger appears you've got options, it says like, you can transform the tiger into a Buddah. You can befriend the tiger or my favorite, you can ride the tiger like a friendly horse. These examples though, were based on the fears of medieval Tibetans, things like high places, 'cause Tibet's really high up, things like fire, a universal fear and things like tigers because Tibet was full of snow leopards. So nowadays, I believe we can absolutely update these teachings and still stay true to their essence. So I ask you, what is your tiger, what is your high place? What is your fire that you can walk into? Working with phobias, working with fears, working with aspects of our shadow absolutely ticks the box of transformation stays dream yoga and is a powerful way to integrate our fears. The reason that integration of fear in the transformation stage comes so early within the teachings, is because the Buddhists believe that fear is one of the greatest blocks to our spiritual growth. It's said that if you can integrate a fear, you'll not only gain back the energy that that fear is made of, but you also remove the block in your psychophysical system that the fear may have created. Before we move on to stages three or four of dream yoga, it's believed that the energy required to do so, is found within the integration of our fear. Stage three of dream yoga is called multiplication. That's a bit of a funny word here, but it's a translation from the original Tibetan. Multiplication stage essentially asks you to do impossible things within the lucid dream. Again, the translation of the Tibetan texts say things like, make one thing many, make many things one. Make small things great, make great things small. Essentially, do impossible stuff in the lucid dream, explore possibility. See what seems to be difficult to do and see what seems to be easy to do. Do things like fly through the sky. Walk through walls, appear in two places at once. Multiply things, change things, transform the actual dreamscape itself. If I were to give you one practice to do, to best sum up stage three dream yoga, it would be walking through a wall. Now walking through a wall in a lucid dream is a very interesting thing. You might think it sounds easy, but give it a shot. Many people find that when they first try to walk through a wall, the wall is actually solid. Let me tell you a story of when I first tried this. Akon Talku Rimbushje is a Tibetan Buddhist Lama, who is the brother of Lama Meshi Rimbushje. A few years ago, Akon Rimbushje died, but before his death, one of the last teachings he gave to me was about multiplication stage in lucid dreaming. A few months earlier, Akon Rimbushje had given me an instruction based on out of body experiences through lucid dreaming. I completed the instruction and went back to him, expecting and hoping for a similarly esoteric instruction. But this time when I said, Rimbushje, what should I do in my next lucid dream? He gave me three words, which for Akon Rimbushje I was actually quite a lot. He said, walk through wall, walk through wall, what does he mean by that? Just walk through a wall. That's like child's play, I want the advanced stuff, I thought. Of course, externally I went, oh yes, Thank you, thank you, thank you, as you do. A week or so later I became lucid and I remember the Lama's instruction. And even though I wasn't that up for it, I thought well, I'd better try it. This is what he told me to do. So I'm in the dream, I see a wall and I thought, okay, I'll walk through the wall. I walk up to the wall, boom, bang, straight in the nose. The pain wakes me up, thinking what? I was fully lucid, I totally knew I was dreaming but how come that wall was solid? A week or so later I became lucid again. And this time, I remembered the dream plan, walk through a wall. So I found a wall in the lucid dream and I went up to it and I knocked on it and it was solid. I thought, I know I'm dreaming right now. Did a reality check, yep, I'm still dreaming. So I know my body's asleep in bed. I know that everything is made in my mind but this wall still seems solid. However, once I started to contemplate it and remind myself that although the wall seemed to be solid, in fact, it was empty of inherent existence. It was the stuff that dreams are made of, suddenly it became less solid and I was able to put my hand through the wall but it wasn't completely non-solid. It had some texture, it felt like kind of wet concrete and as I pushed my hand through, my forearm, all the way up to the shoulder, I had a moment of panic as I thought, wait, what if the wet concrete goes in my mouth? Just as I thought that, just as I had my moment of doubt, the wall re-solidified and suddenly I was stuck halfway in, halfway out the wall and I wake up in panic. Now, isn't that interesting? That first of all, the wall was solid. Then when I minded myself that it was a dreamlike illusion, it became less solid, but when I doubted myself, it re-solidified, what an interesting teaching on the nature of mind. Then, a week or so after that, I get lucid again. Third time lucky, I get lucid, I see the wall. I remind myself, that wall does not exist. Everything is made of mind, nothing has inherent existence. And I ran through the wall to the other and woke up joyfully. I think the best thing about that story, is the teaching that Akon Rimbushje was giving me. I don't think it was just a teaching about multiplication stage dream yoga. I think it's a teaching on how the mind creates solidity. I think that every time you walk through a wall in a lucid dream, you're creating a new habit of mind that allows you to walk through the walls of your own bullshit. Walk through the walls of your own limitation. Walk through the walls of your own fear in the waking state. And that's where the real power lies. I don't think it was a dream yoga technique. I think it was a lucid living technique. The fourth stage of dream yoga is called unification. Unification with what? With our Buddha nature. Buddha nature describes our inherent goodness, our inner divinity, our natural, enlightened essence. And it's said that through the lucid dreaming practices, you can actually tap into and unify with that fully enlightened essence. Within the dream yoga teachings the unification stage practice usually asks that you go into the lucid dream, do a certain Buddhist meditation practice and then unify with your Buddha nature. However, when I show you how to do this practice step by step, I'm going to offer you a totally secular approach that is open to everyone. So what is the unification stage practice look like? You'd become lucid and then in the lucid dream, you would start reciting the mantra associated with a certain Buddha. Now mantra is a term you hear a lot about but what does it mean? Mantra's made up of two syllables, man, which means mind and tra, which means protection. So a mantra means that which protects the mind, a series of sounds, syllables or words, some translatable, some untranslatable that have a protective effect on the mind. So we become lucid and we start reciting the mantra associated with a certain Buddha. Now mantra that a lot of people know, is that of one of the most famous Buddhas within Tibetan Buddism, Chen Rezi or Avalokiteshvara, as he's known in Sanskrit or Quan Ying, as she is known in Chinese Buddhism. Mom mani padme hum, Mom mani padme hum, is the mantra associated with this Buddha. So you'd become lucid and in the lucid dream, you'd start saying, Mom mani padme hum, Mom mani padme hum, Mom mani padme hum, then you would visualize in the sky of the lucid dream, that certain Buddha, the Buddha Chen Rezi. You'd visualize the Buddha up in the sky, in front of you. And in the third stage, you would imagine that your own body in the dream, transforms into the body of the Buddha. So you actually unify with the Buddha, unlocking your own Buddha nature. How does that look? It literally looks like transforming into the Buddha. So when you look down, your human form would have changed and you would be transformed into the form of the Buddha. Everyone on this quest can engage the four stages of dream yoga, for those still working on getting lucid, practice stage one of dream yoga. For those already having lucid dreams, practice stage two of dream yoga. For those with a stabilized practice, move on to stage three. And for those who are ready to wake up to their limitless power, try stage four. I'm going to explore now, exactly how to practice stage four, by offering you a completely secular approach to unification dream yoga. In one way, this is quite an advanced technique, which I usually don't teach publicly, but I feel that to open you up to at least the potential of this, is to plant the seed of possibility. And it's not just a Buddhist thing, unification stage dream yoga can be applied in any tradition. I even tested this myself. I wasn't brought up Christian. I wasn't even christened, but growing up in England, I guess did at least expose me to the tradition of Christianity. The important thing to know, is that this isn't just a Buddhist thing. I've worked with loads of people who've practiced unification practice when they have not been Buddhists. I want to share a case study from a friend of mine who is a full-on Christian. He is a born again Christian, practicing law in America. He emailed me asking, could he use the Tibetan Buddhist dream yoga techniques for his Christian practice. He ended up engaging the three-step unification process but within the Christian tradition. He became lucid, and once lucid, rather than calling out the mantra of the deity or the Buddha, he started singing a hymn to Jesus. The next step, rather than visualizing the Buddha in the sky, he started to call forth Jesus into the dream and the third step, rather than transforming himself into the Buddha, he said, Charlie, I don't know if step three worked. I said well, what happened? And he went well, I was calling Jesus into the dream. And then suddenly my heart opened up, my chest cavity revealed and suddenly, this huge light beamed out of my chest, filling the entire dream with white light and I woke up and bliss, did it work? I said, yeah man, I think that worked. I'm pretty sure you just unified with your divine nature. So let's break down the three-step process of unification. Step one, after you become lucid, recite a prayer, mantra or affirmation associated with the enlightened energy or deity that you wish to unify with. Step two, visualize that enlightened energy or deity within the dream in front of you. Step three, engage self manifestation as the deity, either drawing the energy of that visualized form into you, or trying to transform your own form in the dream into that of the deity. Essentially you want to to unify fully with the enlightened energy of that form, so that you literally become one with them, merging fully into their energy. I know this technique seems super esoteric and in many ways it is, but I really want to plant the seed in your consciousness, just so you see how far these practices can go, to put them in context of the tradition in which they come from and to allow you to see that you can move beyond the limits of what you think is possible in your lucid dreams. As you can see, the dream yoga teachings form part of a powerful tradition. So now it's time for a powerful technique, and one that actually you find within the ancient dream yoga traditions too. Is day 24 of 30 and now is the perfect time to drop this bombshell onto your bed. A technique that can increase your chances of getting lucid by a whopping 2000%, and more than two thirds of research participants recorded lucid dreams when they first engaged this practice. It does require quite a big ask of you but it is definitely worth it. How do we practice this supercharged technique? It's actually simple. You wake up at least two hours earlier than you intend to wake up. You stay awake for about an hour and then you go back to sleep for another hour or two. Here are the five steps to wake back to bed. Step one, set your alarm for at least two hours earlier than your usual wake up time. Step two, when the alarm sounds, wake up, write down your dreams and get out of bed. Step three, stay awake for about an hour, engaging in any fully wakeful activity. Things like meditating or reading about lucid dreaming would be ideal. But I found that just about any mindful activity works well. Stay awake for an hour. Step four, you want to be awake but not wired, so don't overstimulate yourself or you might find step five a bit tricky, try to keep sleep within arms reach. Step five, after about an hour, reset your alarm for an hour or two later, or whenever you intend to wake up, return to bed and fall asleep again with the strong intention to gain lucidity. Of course, you can use one of the hypnagogic affirmation techniques too, that'd work brilliantly. So that's it, but how does the technique work? Where does it get its power? Well, as we've learned, the last two hours of our sleep cycle, are when we do most of our dreaming. So if we starve ourselves of this dream time, when we do go back to sleep eventually, we'll course smoothly and deeply into vivid lucid dreaming. I want you to imagine that your sleep cycle is like a river. It meanders through like this. What we're doing is we're putting a dam in that river. So where's the river usually courses through about to enter dreaming, we put a dam there and the river starts to backup and backup and backup, every minute of every hour that we're awake the brain is going, where are my dreams? Where are my dreams, where are my dreams? Where are my dreams? So when we go back to sleep an hour later and we remove the dam, the block, boom, we get a big flood of dreaming. And as dreaming sleep is the playing field for lucidity, we can see how the power of this technique works. So simply wake up a couple of hours earlier than usual. Stay awake for an hour and then go back to sleep with a strong intention to be lucid. Only use this technique two or three times max a week, otherwise your sleep cycle will just readjust, just like the river will find a new path and the power will decrease. The wake back to bed technique is the golden ticket to lucidity for many people, but sometimes it takes a little bit of tweaking. So for example, if I set my alarm for just two hours early than I intend to wake up, by the time I've stayed awake for an hour, I'm actually too awake and I can't get back to sleep. So when I do wake back to bed, I set my alarm for three hours earlier than I intend to wake up. Then by the time I've done the hour, I could still really do with that extra two hours of sleep. So feel free to adjust your timings. Some people find that a full hour of wakefulness is too much. So make it less, do 45 minutes. Make sure it's at least half an hour though and at most, 90 minutes. Why, because those 90 minute sleep cycles actually continue while we're awake. So by staying awake for an hour and then dropping back the sleep, we're dropping back at a time when we're about to start dreaming. Again, this technique is not based on faith, it's not based on a certain Buddhist mantra. It's based on the power of your sleep cycle and the power of your intent. I really advise you to try wake back to bed technique tonight. Don't do any multiple wake ups when doing this technique, just one wake up is enough. And now it's time for your actions for today. Number one, practice the wake back to bed technique tonight. As you fall asleep, in the wake back to bed technique, feel free to apply one of the hypnagogic affirmation techniques that we learnt before. Secondly, if you become lucid tonight, why not practice one of the stages of dream yoga? It could be transformation of fear. It could be multiplication of the dreamscape. It could even be unification, but give one of those a shot tonight. And as always, keep up the three D's, reality checks and the Colombo mindset. As you can see, we are really taking the safety off this week, as we dive deep into the mystic. Tomorrow, we find out as Neo did in the Matrix, that there is no spoon. Until then, dream deeply and sleep sound.

LESSON 25 – There Is No Spoon: Falling Asleep Consciously
--------------------
<v ->Today, we're going to learn about bendy spoons,</v> impossibility, and how to fall asleep and enter the lucid dream without even blacking out. Hold tight, because today we are going in. You might remember a scene, in the 1999 film, The Matrix, in which Neo is waiting to go in to visit the Oracle. In the waiting room, outside the Oracle's office, Neo watches a weird looking kid who kind of looks a bit like a Shaolin monk, and has a strange English accent, bending a spoon with his mind. Neo watches the kid and says to him, "How are you doing that? "How do you bend the spoon?" To which the kid replies, "Don't try to bend the spoon. "That's impossible. "Instead, only try to realize the truth. "There is no spoon." This idea of there is no spoon is what I use to explore the concept of impossibility in a lucid dream. Although some things in a lucid dream seem to be more difficult than others, such as walking through a wall, compared to walking through a room. In fact, in a lucid dream, the wall and the space in the room are made of the same stuff, nothingness. In a lucid dream, there is no spoon, there is no wall, there is no room. Once you learn about this idea of, there is no spoon, and once you introduce it to your dreamer simply by being aware of it, this is a phrase that you can use in your lucid dream, anytime you're stuck or blocked by your own limitation. For example, let's say you're in a lucid dream and you're trynna fly, but you don't seem to have enough power to take off. Remind yourself, there is no spoon. As in, there is no gravity. There is no body with any weight that would prevent me from flying. There is no spoon means, anything is possible. Or perhaps you're in a lucid dream, you've called forth your shadow and a huge monster has appeared, and you're too scared to hug it. In that case, remind yourself, there is no spoon. As in, there is no shadow. There is no body. There is no monster. Everything is pure possibility. And then of course go and hug it. You can even do the same in your waking life. Anytime in your waking life, you come face to face with your own self limitation, with your own fear, with your own sense of impossibility, you could say to yourself, there is no spoon, and notice the sense of tightness and limitation dissolve. I've used this technique loads of times in the waking state. One of the first times when I was about to go into the ring for a kickboxing competition, I was really scared. It all just seemed too real. My opponent was in front of me. I just found out his name was Tyson Mitchell. That was enough to scare me. In my fear, I thought I can't do this. I can't go into the ring. And then I reminded myself, "Charlie, there is no spoon. "These limits you're putting on yourself are fear created. "You're acting from a place of fear." As soon as I said, it was like something released, something dropped away, and I started to enjoy myself. And I walked into that ring knowing that I was going to have fun. There was no spoon. These limits I put on myself were self-imposed. They had no existing reality. More recently, I use this idea of there is no spoon when I was about to go on stage at the Ministry of Defense to give a talk to 300 MOD staff about the power of lucid dreaming. If you would have told me 10 years ago that I'd be talking about lucid dreaming in the Ministry of Defense I would have told you, "You're dreaming." So before I went on stage, I did have quite a lot of anxiety, and I reminded myself, "Okay, the anxiety is dreamlike. "It's self-imposed, it's a losery. "Charlie, there is no spoon." And once I said it, there is no spoon, something within me released, relaxed. And I realized these limits are self-created, mind-created. They're empty of inherent existence, just like a dream. There is no spoon. When you first hear about this next technique, you might need to have a, there is no spoon moment, because it can seem impossible, but actually it is totally possible to transition from the waking state through the hypnagogic, into dream with no blackout. Let's learn how in falling asleep consciously. For many people, this next technique is the holy grail of lucid dreaming practice. But let's not make such a big deal about it because if we do, we sometimes set up an expectation that can limit us. Although it can take quite a long time to master I've taught hundreds of people this technique and I've seen them apply it successfully within a few weeks, even many on the first night of practice. This technique is called falling asleep consciously. And the aim is to pass through the hypnagogic state into REM dreaming sleep, without blacking out or losing consciousness at all. Falling asleep consciously is both incredibly simple and often incredibly elusive. It involves letting your body and brain fall asleep, while part of your mind stays aware. I absolutely advise you to only practice this technique after awakening in the last few hours of your sleep cycle, when of course you'll enter REM dreaming straight from the hypnagogic state. As we learned before, when you first fall asleep it might take at least 80 minutes before you enter your first dream period. But if you wake yourself in the early hours and then fall back asleep, you can enter dreaming within about 15 minutes. For this technique, the more direct your entry into the dream state and the quicker you can enter it, the better, and the easier it will be to do. To enter the dream state lucidly, you need to be like a surfer. You need to paddle through the hypnogogic imagery and then drop into the wave of lucidity. If you have a good sense of mental balance and awareness then this is the technique for you. Let's look at five steps to falling asleep consciously. Step number one. After at least four and a half hours of sleep wake yourself up and write down your dreams. Then reset your intention to gain lucidity, close your eyes and allow yourself to drift back to sleep through the hypnagogic. Step two. As you enter the hypnagogic state, rather than reciting and affirmation as we've done before, I want you to gently focus your mental awareness on the hypnagogic imagery and simply float through it, be aware of it. Allow the hypnagogic imagery to build, layer upon layer upon layer. The key here is to maintain a delicate vigilance without blacking out and being sucked into the dream state unconsciously. Step three. Don't engage the hypnagogic imagery, but don't reject it either. Just lie there watching the hypnogogic imagery build until the dreamscape has been formed sufficiently for you to drop into it consciously. If you feel yourself blacking out just keep bringing your focus back to the hypnagogic imagery. It will continue to layer upon layer upon layer, until it starts to coalesce into an actual dreamscape. This is a wonderful thing to witness. Step four. As the dreamscape solidifies, you might feel a slight pool or sensation of being sucked forwards. This is an indication that the wave of the dream is now fully formed. In surfing terms, you're on point break. Step five. If you can just stay conscious for a few more moments and are ready to take the plunge, you'll soon find yourself dropping into the wave, with full lucidity. Falling asleep consciously can take quite some practice. But even when it doesn't work, it's still highly beneficial to do, because essentially, it's asking you to meditate into your dreams. So when you practice it, you're meditating into sleep which can affect your entire sleep cycle in a really powerful way. And when it does work it is such a cool experience to go from the waking state into a fully lucid dream without blacking out, that you will never forget it. In the early hours of the morning your hypnagogic state will lead you straight into REM dreaming, but the only way to access dreaming from the hypnagogic state, is to maintain mindful awareness without blacking out. Let's say it's five o'clock in the morning. You've woken up, you've written down your dreams, you go back into the hypnagogic state. At that time in the morning, the hypnogogic imagery will probably start arising quite quickly. So you see the imagery, the faces, the many dreams, the colors, the shapes, and you're just aware of them. You don't grab hold of them 'cause if you do you'll be grabbing hold of dream content and you'll be sucked into a non lucid dream. But if you ignore them or reject them you'll just stay in the waking state. So there's a balance here. To be aware of the hypnagogic, not pushing it away, but also not fully engaging it either. If you can manage to stay in that hypnagogic state consciously, after a few minutes, you'll notice that the quality of the hypnagogic begins to get thicker. It's almost like it's layering, and it is layering. Eventually the hypnagogic imagery will layer and layer and layer until it reaches a critical mass. When that critical mass is met, boom, suddenly a dream appears. And if you can manage to be lucid at that moment without blacking out, you enter the dream state with full lucidity, no need to reality check, no need to find a dream sign because you've gone from the hypnagogic state into the dream with no lack of consciousness. It's well worth trying. It's a really powerful experience. I advise everyone to try this technique. I know it can seem quite testing, but it's got a lot of beginners luck, and some people find they can nail it on their first night of practice. And even if you do think it's too hard, remember nothing's more difficult than anything else, because there is no spoon. And now, your actions for today. Firstly, practice the falling asleep consciously technique, either by itself or combined with the weight back to bed technique that we learned yesterday. Secondly, I want you to share a, there is no spoon moment in your life with the tribe. Perhaps a moment where you broke through a barrier, perhaps a moment where you faced the fear, or you moved beyond the limitation, anything like that, share that with the tribe. Also be sure to practice one of the stages of dream yoga if you become lucid tonight and as always keep up the three Ds, reality checks, and the Columbo mindset. Tomorrow, we continue to dive deep as we look at how lucid dream training has been used by several cultures around the world to train for the moment of death. Until then, enjoy your dreams.

LESSON 26 – Preparing for Death Through Lucid Dreaming
--------------------
<v ->Lucid dreaming is one of the most</v> life affirming practices there is. But today we're going to explore how lucid dreaming can prepare us for death. Now by looking at death, this isn't designed to bum us out. It's actually designed to shake us up and wake us up to the limitations of our own mortality. And to learn how lucid dreaming can help prepare us for the most important moments of our lives, the end of it. Death is a shared commonality of human experience, and yet one that most people never even talk about. Thanatophobia, which is the fear of one's own mortality, is unsurprisingly said to be the most prevalent fear among humans. But as I'm sure you've learned already, this Quest is about facing our fear. A more what better way to do so, than a practice that prepares us to meet that fear head on. Lucid dreaming is said to be one of the most powerful practices to conscious dying. So now let's learn one of the most powerful sleep practices for lucid dreaming. When I was 17, I had a near-death experience. I'd like to say it was one of those special ones where people meet angels and stuff, but actually it was a little bit more raw. I had a drugs overdose that led my heart to stop. I did have the full tunnel of light and the life review, but it was pretty scary. And when I came out surviving the near death experience, I experienced panic attacks and nightmares. In fact, it was those panic attacks that led me to explore meditation because I thought, unless I worked my own mind, I'm going to get sent to a psych ward. And when the nightmares continued, it was that that led me to lucid dreaming. As we learned before, I had been lucid dreaming from the age of 16, 17, but only using it for sex and skateboarding. But once I started to have those nightmares, I realized, maybe lucid dreaming could help me cure them and it did. Four months of post-traumatic stress disorder nightmares ended up being cured in one lucid dream. So it's at 17 that I first discovered the full power of lucid dreaming. And also at 17 that I made the connection between death and dreams. Within the Tibetan Buddhist tradition, lucid dreaming isn't seen as a left field, New Agey practice as it is in the West. Lucid dreaming is seen as one of the most high practices within the whole tradition. This is because it prepares us for the most important moments of our life, our death. If you talk to some Tibetan Lamas about your non lucid dreams, you'll see they tend to glaze over as every Westerner seems to want to tell them about the same dream where they were having tea with the Dalai Lama. But if you start talking to Tibetan lamas about your lucid dreams, you might see that they perk up. Because within the Buddhist tradition, lucid dreaming is seen as a profound practice. And the reason it's seen as such a profound practice is this direct connection to conscious dying. As you might know already, Buddhists believe in rebirth. But it's not as simple as, when you die you're instantly reborn. Within the Tibetan Buddhist tradition, it's believed that when you die, but before you are reborn or reincarnate into your next life, there's a place in between. And this place in between is called the bardo. And although our entire experience of existence is made up of a series of bardos, usually when you hear that term it's referring to the after death bardo. It's believed that when we die, but before we're reborn to our next life, we enter a after death bardo state. That bardo state is said to be dreamlike and hallucinatory. Because as the mind separates from the physical body at the point of death, it flips inwardly and experiences the totality of its own projection, a bit like when we dream. So it's believed that if by the time of death, you have mastered the art of lucid dreaming, the ability to go into your dreams and go, "Aha, I'm dreaming." Then at the point of death, when the dreamlike hallucinations arise you might be able to recognize them as well and go, "Aha, I'm dying." Lucid death. Conscious dying is said to be the highest achievement of the spiritual practitioner. And although the New Age crew like to think otherwise, most people do not die consciously. Why? Because we die as we dream. As my teacher, Lamia Shirimpashe once told me, "Charlie, you want to know "what your mind will be like at death? "Look at how your mind is "during dreams. "If you can reliably train the mind "to recognize your dreams as dreams, "then death means nothing to you. "because at the point of death, "you can recognize the bardo "like a dream "and then you can be with Buddha." So he is directly saying, as all the Tibetan lamas have said in the past, that lucid dreams training is training for the moment of death, the ultimate dream. So let's look at this connection in some more depth. Essentially it's said that dying process mirrors the process of falling asleep. So every time when we fall asleep we're getting a trial run for death and dying. And if we can master the art of falling asleep consciously, maintaining full awareness as we pass through the hypnagogic state into sleep, then we might be able to die consciously too, which is said to be the highest achievement on the spiritual path. Out of all the techniques we've looked at so far, it's said that falling asleep consciously is the most direct training for conscious dying. Because in the same way, as we go from the waking state through the hypnagogic into dream with no blackout, it trains us to go from life, through the dying state, into the bardo, with no blackout. I'm aware that most people watching this Quest are not going to be Buddhists. So please don't think that I'm telling you this to try and push a belief system on you. The reason I'm telling you this is to give you some sort of context as to where lucid dreaming sits within the tradition. So you know that lucid dreaming isn't some New Agey practice that's done just for fun. In this tradition, lucid dreaming has been practiced for hundreds of years, for training for the most important moment of our lives, our deaths. And even if you don't believe in any of this, there will be part of your mind that can't unknow what's just been said. And so hopefully every time you practice lucid dreaming there'll be parts of you that knows you're training for your death and dying states. LESSON 27 – Lucidity Stabilisation Techniques
--------------------
<v ->As we approach the end of week four</v> many of you may have started already having lucid dreams, which is great. And for those of you who haven't, don't worry. The lucidity is coming soon. So now the next thing we need to do is learn how to stay in the lucid dream once we're in it. Welcome to maintaining lucidity techniques. Have you ever been to the circus and looked up at a tightrope walker balancing on the high wire? Maintaining lucidity is exactly the same. It's just like balancing on a tightrope. On one side of a lucidity tightrope is non-lucid dreaming, and on the other side of the lucidity tightrope is the waking state. What we need to do is keep our balance in the middle. When we first step on to a tightrope we might only be able to walk a few steps. Naturally, we've only just stepped on there. We're going to fall off the wire in a few seconds. It's exactly the same when we first start lucid dreaming. As many of you will have found out already, your first few lucid dreams go something like this. "I'm dreaming, I'm dreaming, this is so cool", boom and then we wake up in bed. Yeah, like that. So how do we stop that? How do we make sure that the aha moments of lucid awareness doesn't make us so excited we wake up? But also, the after the aha moments of lucid awareness we don't get so distracted, we become non-lucid. For many beginners the difficulty is not so much getting onto the lucidity spectrum, it's staying there. The aha moments of lucid awareness is often so strong that we lose our balance and fall off the tightrope into the waking state. Or, on the other hand, we can fall the other way into non-lucid dreaming. This is caused by becoming distracted and forgetting that we're dreaming once we're lucid. Here are three techniques to help us learn to stay on the tightrope of lucidity. Technique number one, keep calm and carry on. Keep calm and carry on is pretty good advice for lucid dreamers too, if you're in the lucid dream and you feel that the excitement is waking you up. We need to keep calm and carry on dreaming in order to avoid the panic of waking up too quickly. Essentially, the first part is about doing whatever you would do in the waking state to keep calm, but you do it in the lucid dream. So, as you've seen by now, I'm pretty verbal, pretty vocal, so if I'm in a lucid dream and I feel myself waking up I'll go, "Okay, stay calm Charlie, stay calm, "it's just a lucid dream, stay calm". Other people might like to regulate their breathing as a way to stay calm. Others might like to try self soothing in some way, calming themselves down, stroking themselves gently. However you'd do it in the waking state, do it in the dream state. It'll work in exactly the same way. The second part of the technique is really important, too. Carry on, because the lucid dream likes movement and if we stay too still or prevaricate about what we want to do, we may find that the lucidity begins to slip. Think about it. How many dreams have you had when you're totally still? Most dreams tend to have a sense of movement. You're searching for something. You're being chased by someone. You're looking for something. You're moving. So, if you become lucid and you stand totally still, the dream almost doesn't know what to do with it. It kind of freaks the dream out and you might find you wake up. So, stay calm, but also carry on. The second technique is about engaging your dream plan. This technique seems self explanatory, but actually I was teaching the exact opposite for the first five years of my career. I used to tell people, like most other lucid dreaming teachers still do, that when you first get lucid don't do much. Just stay calm, ground yourself, look around. Actually, what I now realize though is that this wastes precious time and that the lucid dream is like a fire that needs fuel to keep burning. So, if you do too little the fire evaporates. So I say the best way to keep your balance on the lucidity tightrope and the best way of feeding the dream the fuel it needs is to call our your Sankalpa as soon as you become lucid. So as soon as you're lucid do your dream plan. The first time I experienced this I'd become lucid in a dream that was really shaky, really low level lucidity, but my dream plan was really important to me. It was to send healing to my mom who was sick. So, I became lucid and I called out my Sankalpa. "May my mom be happy and well. "May my mom be free from disease." As soon as I called out the Sankalpa it's as if the dreamer responded and voom, suddenly the dream became incredibly solid, incredibly high definition, incredibly vivid. I like to think it was the dreamer going, "Oh wait, this one's really important to Charlie. "This is a really important dream plan for him. "Let's give him a nice, stable, lucid dream." Try it out, see if it works for you. The third technique is my absolute favorite. It's called "Lucidity Boost". Essentially, you're in a lucid dream and you feel the lucidity start to fade or you feel yourself start to wake up and you call out within the lucid dream, "Lucidity Boost!", or "Amplify Lucidity!". And exactly the same way the dreamer responds and gives you what you asked for. I know this can seem weird, but actually think about it. When you get lucid and you call out, inner child come to me, the dreamer responds by presenting you with a 3-D personification of your own inner child. So actually, it doesn't ask much to go, "Hey dreamer, give me some more lucidity". Knock and the door will open. Once we call out lucidity boost, or increase lucidity or something like that, we might see a sudden increase in lucid awareness and a sharpening of the detail in the dreamscape as the dreamer manifests our request. It's really quite powerful the first time you do it, to see how there's like a bio feedback mechanism working. You call out for it. The dreamer responds. You call out for it. The dreamer responds. This technique is so cool that I sometimes like to try it out in the waking state when I'm feeling dull or sleepy. So, in the same way, as we learned before, that we can use the, there is no spoon affirmation in the waking state, if we're feeling scared, nervous or limited. If you're feeling dull or sleepy in the waking state, call out "Lucidity boost!". See if it works. It really does for me. And now, your actions for today. Number one, practice one of the lucidity maintenance techniques once you become lucid. So, even if your dreamer's super stable why not try out something like Lucidity Boost just to see if you can get it even more lucid? Of course, tonight practice one of the chosen techniques, either Falling Asleep Consciously, Wake, Back to Bed, or one of the four Hypnogogic Affirmation techniques. As always, keep up the 3 Ds, the reality checks and the Columbo mindset. And also, don't forget to check in with your Tribe dream circles. Keep that going. Check in, share your dreams, share your experiences, ghee each other up, move towards lucidity together. Tomorrow as per usual, we will be having our weekly pep talk and our weekly quiz. But, we won't be having our bedtime story. Booo. But, don't worry, we're going to have something even more fun. We are going to conduct a lucid dream psychic communication experiment. You're going to love it. Until then, sweet dreams.

Week 5: Going Further

LESSON 29 – Overcoming 5 Common Obstacles to Lucid Dreaming
--------------------
<v ->Before we get to our main lesson today,</v> we, of course, need to put your minds to rest by telling you what the target image was from last night's psychic experiment. So now that all the answers are in, I can tell you the target image was a hummingbird. This particular hummingbird, in fact. This is the exact image that I used. So obviously, if your answer was hummingbird, then well done, you absolutely smashed the ball out of the park on this psychic communication exercise. But for those of you who didn't get it, maybe you came close. Our psychic sixth sense is, for most modern humans anyway, the most underused of our senses and thus, it can be a bit rusty. So give yourself a break. Like if you got the answer as a bird, for example, but not hummingbird, that's still amazingly good. Or if you tune into the dominant color of green, then that'd be pretty good too. Or maybe it was more abstract, like maybe you thought the target image was sequined pink hot pants. Now, that could actually be a pretty close hit because look at one of the most striking parts of this image is the bright pink area of the hummingbird's throat feathers. Sometimes our mind interprets it in slightly more abstract ways. For other people, it's slightly more literal. Anyway, that was just for a bit of fun as we approach the end of our Quest together. So don't sweat it too much. We are here primarily to learn how to lucid dream, not to prove the interconnected quantum entanglement of all reality. But if you did manage to do so by getting the answer as hummingbird, then I absolutely salute you. So now onto our lesson for today. And we begin with a quote. A Buddhist master once told me, "Obstacles are not things that block the path, "obstacles are the path." And never was this truer than on the path of lucid dreaming. Today we are going to troubleshoot some of the most common gremlins that you are likely to encounter on your lucid dreaming path. And how do we deal with gremlins? In exactly the same way as we deal with our shadow. We love them and we hug them. One of the most common gremlins for lucid dreamers to encounter is that as soon as they become lucid in their dream, they wake up. That's really common and remember, we've now got our three practices to help maintain our lucidity and to stay calm without falling off the tightrope. Even with those techniques though, you can find this gremlin popping up again and again but know this, eventually it gets easier. Every time you step onto that tightrope, your balance gets better and better and better. I actually have fewer lucid dreams nowadays than I used to have 10 years ago. But 10 years ago, my lucid dreams were really short 'cause my balance wasn't that good. Nowadays, I might have fewer lucid dreams but when I'm in the lucid dream, I'm in there for a long period of time because my balances got better. You'll find the same thing happens with you, although I hope your frequency stays high too. Another gremlin that some people find is that when they wake up to do one of the lucid dreaming techniques in the middle of the night, they can't get back to sleep. We mentioned before that if you're working with anxiety and depression, this might be a gremlin you encounter but just for the record, anyone who finds they can't get back to sleep after awakening for a lucid dreaming technique, just feel free to concentrate on the daytime practices, like reality checks, Columbo method, looking for dream signs, rather than nighttime wake-up practices. "I've lost my motivation." That's a big gremlin. As soon as we finish this Quest, after you feel the support of the Tribe members reduce slightly, you might start to encounter this. You might be asking yourself, "Why am I lucid dreaming? "What am I doing this for? "Why am I waking up at four o'clock in the morning?" When that happens, remind yourself of your reason why. Go back to your dream plan and check your dream plan is still turning you on psychologically and still making you salivate. And if it isn't, write a new dream plan. Remember, your dream plan should make you so excited that you want to go to bed early because you can't wait to engage the dream plan. Some people find that they don't know what technique to choose. This is because there are so many different lucid dreaming techniques to choose from but actually, within this Quest, I have intentionally limited our techniques. You'll soon find out there are dozens more we haven't even mentioned. But the reason I've limited our techniques are because I've chosen the ones that really work well for the most amount of people. So you've got effective techniques. You just need to decide on which technique to use, and stick to that. In fact, you only need to learn one lucid dreaming technique if one lucid dreaming technique works for you. But you might need to explore half a dozen or so until you find that one that really works. And the final gremlin is pure fantasy, I promise you. "I just can't do it." The inability to have lucid dreams is as much of an illusion as the dreams themselves. Remember, you were lucid dreaming as a child. In fact, you don't need to learn how to lucid dream, you just need to remember how. Think about a language that you used to speak as a child but no longer speak. How much easier it would be to remember that language from childhood than to learn a new language from scratch. It's the same with lucid dreaming. Something in you remembers how to lucid dream. So you don't need to learn lucid dreaming, you just need to remember how. And of course, don't forget, even if you manage to get lucid every night of your life, still 80% of your lucid dream experience will be non-lucid. So for those of us who are hitting a pretty good average of maybe one lucid dream a week, then 97% of your dream experience will be non-lucid. So if we only focus on the lucid dreams, we are rejecting 97% of our experience. Don't do that. We want to open ourselves up to the entire experience of dreaming to that whole third of our life that we spend sleeping. Lucid dreaming is great. It's like a peak experience but don't focus so much on that peak experience that you lose the focus on the rest of your dream life. I think one of the major problems with lucid dreaming is the fact that it's called lucid dreaming. The aim is in the name, which leads some people to think that if they are not having lucid dreams, they're outright failures because they're not achieving this aim. Let me clarify. In the game of baseball, the aim is to score home runs. But the name of baseball isn't home run ball, it's baseball, why? Because baseball is about proceeding through the bases, not just about hitting home runs. It's the same with lucid dreaming. Just as you can be a great baseball player without having hit many home runs, you can also be a great lucid dreaming practitioner without actually having had many lucid dreams. How can that be? Well, you might be a great hypnagogic mindfulness meditator or you might have amazing ability to recall your dreams or you might be brilliant at decoding meaning from your dreams. All of these are great attributes to have on the playing field of lucidity and yet none of them are determined by having a single lucid dream. A lady on my first course once told me, "I haven't even had a lucid dream yet. "But I can say for sure that the act "of sleeping will never be the same again "and in that way, it changed my life. "I can't unknow what I've learnt "about the sleep stages, about the process of dreaming, "about the hypnagogic states. "Falling asleep will never be the same again." For me, the experience of that student was actually more important than the experience of the others who had loads of lucid dreams because it showed me that the process of learning how to bring awareness into the dream and sleep states had profoundly changed a lifetime of habitual tendency, something which is far more beneficial than having a one-off lucid dream. The most important thing to remember is this. Don't just fall asleep. Try and do something, however small, each an every night. If you're struggling with lucidity, then train in the daytime with your reality checks. If you're struggling with dream recall, then work on the Columbo method. If you're too tired to stay awake at night, then take a nap and do your hypnagogic mindfulness during the day. There are so many ways in which you can engage this practice, so don't put it off. Do something today. Make a change, however small. Make your next sleep the first sleep of the rest of your life. And now your actions for today. Firstly, practice either the falling asleep consciously technique, the Wake, Back to Bed technique, or one of the four hypnagogic affirmation techniques. Secondly, keep up the 3 Ds, the reality checks, and the Columbo mindset as per usual. And finally, share one of your personal gremlins with the Tribe. If you can be vulnerable enough to share one of your gremlins, you might allow another Tribe member to open up about something that's been on their mind too. Our lucid dreaming gremlins should be dealt with in exactly the same way as we deal with our lucid dreaming shadow, with love and hugs. Embrace your fears and you set them free. So be sure to embrace your gremlins. I look forward to seeing you tomorrow for day 30, the final day of our Quest. Until then, sweet dreams.

====================

LESSON 30 – Continuing Your Lucid Dreaming Journey
--------------------
<v ->Today, we're going to review</v> and recap the entire 30 day course. But before we do that, congratulations on completing the Quest. Whether you're already having loads of lucid dreams or whether you are still yet to have a lucid dream on day 30, the fact is, you've done what most people never do. You have allowed yourself to wake up to the potential of waking up in your dreams. You have opened yourself up to 30 years of experience that most people never experience, that in itself is worth congratulating. So from my dream and to yours, I salute you. So in week one, we covered the basics, what lucid dreaming is and how it works. We looked at all the brain studies into lucid dreaming, how it can be used to work with trauma, how athletes use it to prepare for their athletic disciplines and also the top five why's of lucid dreaming. It could be a really good idea now that you're at the end of the Quest to go back and revisit week one. Remind yourself, how is it that the brain can change in response to a lucid dream. If you're stuck for a dream plan, go back and watch the 10 top why's of lucid dreaming. Now, I only gave you 10 but I could have given you 50 and I'm sure that you could go back there and think of 40 more amazing reasons why to have a lucid dream so don't feel limited by that top 10 but allow them to re-inspire you anytime you need to write a new dream plan. Week one was also when we did the guided meditation, Meeting Your Dreamer. Now that you completed the Quest, it could be a really good idea to go back and do that meditation again because your relationship with your dreamer will have changed so drastically from day one to day 30. Week two was all about making friends with our dreams. The main thing there was dream signs, dream decoding and looking at the sleep cycles. Now if there's anything around the science of sleep that you're still unsure about, go back and re-watch that video. We also looked at dream decoding, which although we pushed quite hard and we two, we didn't actually revisit that much so if you like go back and remind yourself how you can decode meaning from your dreams, lucid or non. Now week three was when we really started cooking. This is when we looked at reality checks, the weird technique and hypnagogic affirmation. Those are like the core techniques within all the lucid dream traditions. Basically checking awareness during the day leading to boosted awareness at night and falling asleep with a strong intention to gain lucidity. If there's anything in week three that you're still unsure about, make sure you go back and watch those videos again, but week three forms the center of our entire practice. The title of week four comes from a quote by the famous mythologist Joseph Campbell, the cave you fear to enter holds the treasure that you see. And in week four, we really did start to enter that cave, as we looked at the shadow, nightmares, how we can become lucid within them, we explored the idea of the golden shadow which for many people might have been a new concept so make sure you go back and really get to grips with that and if you haven't already, make a dream plan to meet your golden shadow in your lucid dream. Week four was about building upon the firm foundation that were created in week one, two and three. It was when we really started to learn how to lucid dream and open ourselves up to the full potential of lucid dreaming and Dream Yoga, not only as a psychophysical practice, but also as a spiritual one. Week four also contained the guided meditation, Circle of Protectors. I know at the time it was presented mainly as a practice to do if you're currently working with nightmares, but it's actually a really good practice to do day or night and a really good way to rejuvenate your lucid dream practice if you feel it's getting a bit dull, so don't forget about the Circle of Protectors guided meditation. And finally, in week four we had the lucidity stabilization techniques, things you can do in the lucid dream to make the lucid dream last longer, you're going to need to keep returning to those when you start to have a lot of lucid dreams because when we first step onto that tightrope it can be a bit wobbly but remember with practice it gets easier and easier. And now week five, where we've looked at greeting gremlins, and how to move forward on the path. Remember, this lucid dreaming Quest does not end today at day 30, today is when it begins. It begins when you go back to your bed without the support of the Tribe Quest members. When you go back to your bed without the support of the daily videos, when you go back to your bed just you and your dreamer and the strong intention to change your life for your dreams. Just because the Quest has come to an end, it doesn't mean you have to end your dream circles with the Tribe, keep it up. Keep sharing your dreams, keep sharing your experiences and share them with me too. If you want even send me a 4 am email when you have your first lucid dream. Now I want to explore how to continue your journey by breaking this down into daily tasks, weekly tasks, monthly tasks and lifetime tasks. Let's begin with our daily tasks. Dream diaries need to become your new normal, something you do every single morning. It needs to become as habitual as brushing your teeth. You wake up, you reach for the dream diary, whether it's in your phone or in a notepad but whatever it is, you write something down every single morning. And remember, even if you can't remember your dreams, write that too. It's still totally fine to write, "I know I was dreaming but I can't remember what about". It's authentic, it's mindful, and it keeps up that daily connection with your inner dreamer. Reality checks and the weird technique. I've said it before and I'll say it again, if your friends or loved ones have not been asking what's wrong with your hands, you have not been doing it enough. Now the hand checks that we looked at were things like this. But if you want to change that, feel free to do so, adding a finger pull, adding a gravity check, see if you can fly. You can even pinch your nose to see if you can breathe through it. You'll find loads of different variations of reality checks online, we only looked at a few. It doesn't matter which one you use, it just matters that you use it and it works for you. The handshake however, does become like the secret handshake of the lucid dreamer. I have a fantasy that when lucid dreaming has become so mainstream everyone practices it, there'll be some times perhaps around Halloween, where you'll be on a bus and someone will get on dressed as a monster and the whole bus will go, reality check. Whether that happens or not, I want that to happen to you every time you see something weird. Don't let life pass you by, when something dreamlike happens, come into the present moment and ask yourself, "Am I awake or am I dreaming?" As your weekly schedule, add say maybe three times a week, you could be formally practicing either the hypnagogic affirmation one of the three variations, Falling Asleep Consciously or Wake, Back to Bed. Remember, with Wake, Back to Bed, don't practice it more than two or three times a week. Wake, Back to Bed is based upon creating a change in the normal flow of your sleep cycle so if you were to do it every night, the flow would just adjust and the technique would stop working. Of course, if you want to formally practice lucid dreaming more than three times a week, great. I'm just saying minimum to have a solid practice three nights a week, you need to be doing a formal lucid dreaming technique. As far as your dream plans go, you're not only going to have one lucid dream in your life, you're going to have loads of lucid dreams, you've probably already started having loads of lucid dreams so you already have had multiple dream plans. Whenever you've completed your dream plan, or perhaps you just feel it's not turning you on psychologically anymore, be sure to create a new dream plan. But anytime you have a lucid dream, you need to know this is the dream plan I'm focusing on, what you don't want to happen is that you get into the lucid dream and you're thinking, what's my dream plan? Which one should I do? You'll probably wake up before you've decided so make sure you know what your dream plan is and when you're going to practice it. The multiple wake ups technique is a totally optional practice but if you are into it then maybe once a week, you could schedule in a lucid dreaming date night, making sure you don't have to wake up too early the next day, you could go to bed early, you could set three or four alarms during the night and really go for it so that once a week you're sure you're getting into that lucid dream state to have a fully lucid dream. As far as the dream decoding goes, I know it's not a formal lucid dreaming practice but it's a pretty good idea to try and decode at least one dream a week just to keep the sword sharp and to keep your awareness and connection with the dreamer type. Remember, every time you decode a dream, it may not lead to more lucid dreams but it does lead to more friendship with a dreamer and that will lead to more lucidity. As for your monthly tasks, what I suggest is that once a month you check in with the other Tribe members from the lucid dreaming Quest for a monthly dream circle. This can be done informally or formally. You could choose a few other members and decide to meet on Zoom on a certain evening or a certain day and discuss how is my last month been in relation to dreaming and lucid dreaming. This is a great way to reconnect and to keep the practice fresh. And then personally at the end of every month, I like to flick through my dream diary and just see how this last month been, how many lucid dreams have I managed to have? Have I seen any increases in dream signs or decreases in dream signs? And crucially, how connected have been to my dream practice this month? Sometimes I look at my month and go, that was great, I had loads of lucid dreams, I feel really connected to my dreamer. Other times I look at it and go, God, how could this month happen? I've hardly written down any dreams, I've had no lucidity, what am I doing? When that happens, be kind to yourself, know that life happens regardless of your dream yoga practice. So if you have a bad month, come back to the practice and know that the next month is the first month of the rest of your life. And lifetime tasks, the lifetime task of the lucid dreamer is to continue to connect to the third of your life that you spend sleeping, and to embrace that 30 years with lucidity and enthusiasm. Like all lifetime relationships, it'll move, it'll flash, it'll fade but you can always come back to the fact that you're dreaming every single night and that your dream is always with you from the day that you're born to the day that you die, connection to the dreamer is your birthright. In the first week of the Quest, we did a guided meditation practice called Meeting the Dreamer. Now that we're at the end of our Quest, we're going to do a similar guided meditation but this one's called Thanking the Dreamer. Over the past 30 days, our dream has shown us patience, friendship and lucidity. So this guided meditation is about saying thank you to the dreamer, and continuing our path in gratitude and mutual respect. There's a Buddhist meditation practice called Dedication. Within the Buddhist tradition, it's believed that whenever we do something for the benefit of others, like learning the lucid dream, we create beneficial energy. And any time we create beneficial energy, it's a really good idea to dedicate it which is like sealing the pot, it's sealing in all the power of the energy, allowing us to dedicate the energy to our own spiritual growth and for the spiritual growth of others. Whether you're Buddhist or not, it's a really good idea to dedicate energy so I'd like to guide us in a final dedication now. Just sitting, eyes open or closed, either is fine. Breathing through the nose or the mouth, either is fine. Feeling support of the ground beneath you and noticing your breath flowing in and out of your body. And now I want you to bring your attention to your feet and your legs. Take a moment to really feel into your feet and your legs. Take a moment to say thank you to your feet and your legs. And now shifting our focus to our hands and our arms. We take a moment to say thank you to our hands and our arms. Allowing our awareness to spread through the core of our body, our digestive system, our respiratory system, our ever beating heart. Take a moment to say thank you to the core of your body. And allowing our awareness to flow upwards so that we're now aware from the tips of our toes to the crown of our head. Take a moment to consider your body and take a moment to say thank you. Over the past 30 days, our body has stayed alive. Over the past 30 days, we didn't die. Your body is amazing. We say thank you to our body for staying alive while we engage this Quest. From our bodies to our minds, take a moment to consider your mind. Over the past 30 days of this Quest, We didn't lose it, it's still here. We say key to our mind. From our bodies to our minds, to the dreamer that resides within. Over the past 30 days, our dreamer has engaged deeply with these practices so we take a moment to say thank you to our dreamer now. Thank you to our body, thank you to our mind, thank you to our dreamer for engaging this Quest. in gratitude we ask that our dreamer stays open and aware, ready to guide us deeper into the depths of lucid dreaming. We say thank you for the nightmares that offer such guidance. Thank you for the dreams of insight. Thank you for the lucid dreams that we've had, and for the lucid dreams that we are yet to have. Thank you to our dreamer. Showing gratitude to our body, gratitude to our mind, and gratitude to our dreamer. Finally, we show gratitude to the great dream. The shared dream of waking life, to the other dream characters To the other aspects of this dream that we share this life with. We show gratitude. We show thanks. May we all wake up from the dream together and learn to be more awake, more aware and more kind. To live lucidly, to die lucidly, to dream lucidly. I want you to take a moment to think about the last 30 days that we've spent together, the things that we've learned, the subjects we've explored, the meditation that we've shared. Without doubt you have generated beneficial energy so we take a moment now to dedicate that beneficial energy, not only to ourselves but to all beings, may we all wake up from the dream together, using lucid dreaming to be more awake, more aware, and more kind for the benefit of all living beings. So now there's only one thing left for me to say, good night. I wish you lucid dreams and a lucid life.
